Rule Generator Message:
**Start of Rules**

- **Rule 1**: Modifiers that are α-amino acids with two carboxylic acid groups (e.g., aspartic acid, glutamic acid) lead to **high yield**.
  
- **Rule 2**: Modifiers that are heteroaromatic carboxylic acids containing nitrogen in the ring (e.g., pyridine-3-carboxylic acid, 1H-pyrazole-4-carboxylic acid, 2-oxo-1H-pyridine-4-carboxylic acid) lead to **high yield**.
  
- **Rule 3**: Modifiers that are benzoic acids substituted at the para position with electron-withdrawing groups (e.g., nitro, bromo) lead to **high yield** (e.g., 4-nitrobenzoic acid, 4-(bromomethyl)benzoic acid).

- **Rule 4**: Modifiers that are benzoic acids substituted at the para position with electron-donating groups (e.g., methyl, hydroxy, amino) lead to **low yield** (e.g., 4-methylbenzoic acid, 4-hydroxybenzoic acid derivatives).

- **Rule 5**: Modifiers that are aliphatic carboxylic acids with terminal hydroxyl or amino groups (e.g., 7-hydroxyheptanoic acid, 8-aminooctanoic acid, 7-aminoheptanoic acid) lead to **low yield**.

- **Rule 6**: Modifiers that are aliphatic carboxylic acids without additional polar functional groups (e.g., octanoic acid) lead to **high yield**.

- **Rule 7**: Modifiers containing multiple hydroxyl groups adjacent to the carboxylic acid (e.g., pentahydroxyhexanoic acid, 2-[[1,3-dihydroxy-2-(hydroxymethyl)propan-2-yl]oxy]acetic acid) lead to **high yield**.

- **Rule 8**: Modifiers with perfluorinated alkyl chains (e.g., 2,2,3,3,4,4,5,5,5-nonafluoropentanoic acid) lead to **low yield**.

- **Rule 9**: Modifiers with aldehyde groups attached to the aromatic ring (e.g., 4-formylbenzoic acid, 5-formyl-2-hydroxybenzoic acid) lead to **low yield**.

- **Rule 10**: Modifiers that are aromatic acids directly connected to heteroaromatic rings (e.g., 4-pyridin-4-ylbenzoic acid) lead to **low yield**.

- **Rule 11**: Modifiers that are aromatic acids connected via an aliphatic linker to heteroaromatic rings (e.g., 4-pyridin-4-ylbutanoic acid) lead to **high yield**.

- **Rule 12**: Modifiers with sulfur-containing groups attached to aliphatic chains near the carboxylic acid (e.g., 3-sulfanylpropanoic acid) lead to **high yield**.

- **Rule 13**: Modifiers with sulfur-containing groups directly attached to aromatic rings (e.g., 4-sulfanylbenzoic acid) lead to **low yield**.

- **Rule 14**: Modifiers that are amino acids with long aliphatic chains (more than five carbons) and terminal amino groups (e.g., 7-aminoheptanoic acid, 8-aminooctanoic acid) lead to **low yield**.

- **Rule 15**: Modifiers containing hydroxyl or methoxy groups adjacent to aromatic rings connected via an aliphatic chain (e.g., 5-(4-hydroxyphenyl)pentanoic acid) lead to **high yield**.

**End of Rules**

Rule Generator Message:
**Start of Rules**

- **Rule 1**: **High Yield** is observed with modifiers that are α-amino acids possessing side chains with carboxylic acid groups, such as aspartic acid and glutamic acid (e.g., (2S)-2-aminobutanedioic acid, (2S)-2-aminopentanedioic acid).

- **Rule 2**: Modifiers that are heteroaromatic carboxylic acids containing nitrogen within the ring structure, and not directly fused to another aromatic ring, lead to **High Yield** (e.g., pyridine-3-carboxylic acid, 1H-pyrazole-4-carboxylic acid, 2-oxo-1H-pyridine-4-carboxylic acid).

- **Rule 3**: **High Yield** results from modifiers that are benzoic acids substituted at the para position with strong electron-withdrawing groups such as nitro or bromomethyl groups (e.g., 4-nitrobenzoic acid, 4-(bromomethyl)benzoic acid).

- **Rule 4**: Modifiers that are benzoic acids substituted at the para position with aldehyde groups lead to **Low Yield** (e.g., 4-formylbenzoic acid, 5-formyl-2-hydroxybenzoic acid).

- **Rule 5**: **High Yield** is achieved with aliphatic carboxylic acids that have a straight-chain alkyl group longer than six carbons and lack additional polar functional groups (e.g., octanoic acid).

- **Rule 6**: Modifiers that are aliphatic carboxylic acids featuring terminal hydroxyl or amino groups result in **Low Yield** (e.g., 7-hydroxyheptanoic acid, 7-aminoheptanoic acid, 8-aminooctanoic acid).

- **Rule 7**: Modifiers containing multiple hydroxyl groups directly adjacent to the carboxylic acid group lead to **High Yield** due to increased hydrogen bonding capability (e.g., (2R,3S,4R,5R)-2,3,4,5,6-pentahydroxyhexanoic acid).

- **Rule 8**: Modifiers with perfluorinated alkyl chains exhibit **Low Yield** likely due to their electron-withdrawing nature and steric bulk (e.g., 2,2,3,3,4,4,5,5,5-nonafluoropentanoic acid).

- **Rule 9**: **High Yield** is observed with modifiers containing thiol groups attached to primary alkyl chains near the carboxylic acid (e.g., 3-sulfanylpropanoic acid). In contrast, sulfur-containing modifiers with more complex structures or additional functional groups may lead to **Low Yield**.

- **Rule 10**: Modifiers where aromatic acids are connected to heteroaromatic rings via an aliphatic linker yield a **High Yield** (e.g., 4-pyridin-4-ylbutanoic acid). Conversely, those directly connected without a linker lead to **Low Yield** (e.g., 4-pyridin-4-ylbenzoic acid).

- **Rule 11**: Modifiers that are α-amino acids with aromatic side chains result in **Low Yield** unless the aromatic side chain contains an indole moiety, which leads to **High Yield** (e.g., tryptophan leads to high yield, whereas phenylalanine leads to low yield).

- **Rule 12**: **High Yield** is associated with modifiers having hydroxyphenyl groups connected via aliphatic chains longer than three carbons (e.g., 5-(4-hydroxyphenyl)pentanoic acid). Shorter chains result in **Low Yield** (e.g., 3-(4-hydroxyphenyl)propanoic acid).

- **Rule 13**: Modifiers bearing aldehyde and hydroxyl groups on adjacent positions of an aromatic ring lead to **Low Yield** due to potential interference with catalyst activity (e.g., 5-formyl-2-hydroxybenzoic acid).

- **Rule 14**: Modifiers containing carboxylic acid groups attached to benzofuran or benzofuranone systems promote **High Yield**, possibly due to chelating ability or π-conjugation (e.g., 1,3-dioxo-2-benzofuran-5-carboxylic acid).

- **Rule 15**: Modifiers with polyether chains (e.g., polyethylene glycol-like structures) attached to carboxylic acids result in **Low Yield**, likely due to steric hindrance and solubility effects (e.g., 2-[2-(2-methoxyethoxy)ethoxy]acetic acid).

Rule Generator Message:
**Start of Rules**

- **Rule 1**: Modifiers that are **α-amino acids with side chains containing carboxylic acid groups** (dicarboxylic amino acids) result in **High Yield**. Examples include aspartic acid and glutamic acid (e.g., *(2S)-2-aminobutanedioic acid*, *(2S)-2-aminopentanedioic acid*).

- **Rule 2**: Modifiers that are **heteroaromatic carboxylic acids containing nitrogen atoms within a single ring**, not fused to other rings, lead to **High Yield**. This includes compounds like pyridine and pyrazole carboxylic acids (e.g., *pyridine-3-carboxylic acid*, *1H-pyrazole-4-carboxylic acid*, *2-oxo-1H-pyridine-4-carboxylic acid*).

- **Rule 3**: Modifiers that are **benzoic acids substituted at the para position with strong electron-withdrawing groups** such as nitro or bromomethyl groups result in **High Yield** (e.g., *4-nitrobenzoic acid*, *4-(bromomethyl)benzoic acid*). In contrast, para-substitution with aldehyde groups leads to **Low Yield** (e.g., *4-formylbenzoic acid*).

- **Rule 4**: Modifiers that are **benzoic acids substituted at the para position with electron-donating methyl groups** result in **Low Yield** (e.g., *4-methylbenzoic acid*), whereas substitution with amino groups leads to **High Yield** (e.g., *4-aminobenzoic acid*). Therefore, the nature of the substituent significantly affects the yield.

- **Rule 5**: **Aliphatic carboxylic acids with long, straight-chain alkyl groups (eight carbons or more) and no additional polar functional groups** lead to **High Yield** (e.g., *octanoic acid*). The addition of polar groups like hydroxyl or amino groups at the terminal position reduces the yield, resulting in **Low Yield** (e.g., *7-hydroxyheptanoic acid*, *7-aminoheptanoic acid*, *8-aminooctanoic acid*).

- **Rule 6**: Modifiers containing **multiple hydroxyl groups adjacent to the carboxylic acid group** produce a **High Yield**, possibly due to increased hydrogen-bonding capabilities (e.g., *(2R,3S,4R,5R)-2,3,4,5,6-pentahydroxyhexanoic acid*).

- **Rule 7**: Modifiers with **perfluorinated alkyl chains** exhibit **Low Yield**, likely due to their strong electron-withdrawing effects and steric hindrance (e.g., *2,2,3,3,4,4,5,5,5-nonafluoropentanoic acid*).

- **Rule 8**: **Aliphatic thiol-containing modifiers** near the carboxylic acid group lead to **High Yield** (e.g., *3-sulfanylpropanoic acid*), whereas **aromatic thiol-containing modifiers** result in **Low Yield** (e.g., *4-sulfanylbenzoic acid*).

- **Rule 9**: **α-Amino acids with aromatic side chains** show different yields based on the aromatic group: **indole-containing side chains** (e.g., tryptophan) result in **High Yield** (*e.g., (2R)-2-amino-3-(1H-indol-3-yl)propanoic acid*), while **phenyl-containing side chains** (e.g., phenylalanine) lead to **Low Yield** (*e.g., (2S)-2-amino-3-phenylpropanoic acid*).

- **Rule 10**: Modifiers with **hydroxyphenyl groups connected via aliphatic chains longer than three carbons** to the carboxylic acid result in **High Yield** (e.g., *5-(4-hydroxyphenyl)pentanoic acid*). If the aliphatic chain is three carbons or shorter, the yield is **Low Yield** (e.g., *3-(4-hydroxyphenyl)propanoic acid*).

- **Rule 11**: Modifiers that are **aromatic carboxylic acids connected to heteroaromatic rings via an aliphatic linker** yield a **High Yield** (e.g., *4-pyridin-4-ylbutanoic acid*). If the aromatic rings are directly connected without an aliphatic linker, they result in **Low Yield** (e.g., *4-pyridin-4-ylbenzoic acid*).

- **Rule 12**: Modifiers containing **carboxylic acid groups attached to benzofuran or benzofuranone systems** promote **High Yield**, possibly due to their chelating abilities or extended π-conjugation (e.g., *1,3-dioxo-2-benzofuran-5-carboxylic acid*).

- **Rule 13**: Modifiers with **aldehyde and hydroxyl groups on adjacent positions of an aromatic ring** lead to **Low Yield**, potentially due to interactions that interfere with the catalyst (e.g., *5-formyl-2-hydroxybenzoic acid*).

- **Rule 14**: Modifiers with **polyether chains attached to carboxylic acids** result in **Low Yield**, likely due to steric hindrance and solubility issues (e.g., *2-[2-(2-methoxyethoxy)ethoxy]acetic acid*).

- **Rule 15**: Modifiers that are **peptides or contain amide bonds derived from amino acids with sulfur-containing side chains** lead to **Low Yield** (e.g., *2-[[(2S)-2-amino-4-methylsulfanylbutanoyl]amino]ethanoic acid*).

**Analysis of Current Rule Performance:**

The current rules exhibit notable improvements in certain metrics compared to the reference metrics, particularly in terms of **confidence** and **lift**. Here's a detailed breakdown:

1. **Train Confidence:**
   - **Current:** **1.0**
   - **Reference:** Approximately **0.635** and **0.54**
   - **Interpretation:** Achieving a confidence of 1.0 signifies that every instance where the rule conditions are met in the training data leads to a correct prediction. This is a significant improvement over the previous average confidences and indicates that the rules are highly precise on the training set.

2. **Train Lift:**
   - **Current:** **1.9997**
   - **Reference:** Approximately **1.2048** and **1.0559**
   - **Interpretation:** A lift near 2.0 implies that the rule is twice as effective at predicting the outcome compared to random chance, given the base rate of the outcome in the data. This substantial increase demonstrates that the current rules have a strong association with the desired predictions and are more informative than the previous rules.

3. **Train Support:**
   - **Current:** **0.0571** (approximately **5.71%**)
   - **Reference:** Approximately **16.67%** and **18.45%**
   - **Interpretation:** The support has decreased significantly, indicating that the current rules apply to a much smaller portion of the training data. While high confidence and lift are desirable, the low support suggests that the rules may be too specific, capturing only niche scenarios.

4. **Train Leverage:**
   - **Current:** **0.0286**
   - **Reference:** Approximately **0.0289** and **-0.0041**
   - **Interpretation:** The positive leverage indicates a stronger association than expected by chance, similar to the higher end of the previous leverage values. This suggests that, despite the lower support, the rules contribute additional predictive power beyond random chance.

**Assessment of Local Minimum and Iterations:**

The current metrics indicate that the rules have reached a point of **high precision but low generality**:

- **High Confidence (Precision):** The rules are highly accurate on the training data.
- **Low Support (Generality):** The rules apply to a limited subset of data, which may not be representative of the broader dataset.

This balance suggests that the algorithm may have converged to a **local minimum**, optimizing for confidence and lift at the expense of support. The absence of test metrics in the current iteration prevents a full assessment of whether the rules generalize well to unseen data.

Considering the above, it seems that while the current rules are performing exceptionally on the training data, they might be overfitting and not capturing the underlying patterns applicable to a wider range of data. Therefore, additional iterations focused on improving support without significantly compromising confidence and lift are necessary.

**Recommendations for Improving Current Rules:**

To enhance the performance and applicability of the current rules, consider the following:

1. **Balance Specificity and Generality:**
   - **Adjust Rule Granularity:** Simplify overly specific rules to cover a broader range of scenarios. For example, combining similar rules or identifying common structural features that encompass multiple specific cases can increase support.
   - **Avoid Overfitting:** Ensure that the rules are not tailored too closely to the training data's idiosyncrasies. Introduce some flexibility to account for variations in the data.

2. **Incorporate Test Metrics:**
   - **Evaluate on Test Data:** Calculate the confidence, lift, support, and leverage on a test dataset to assess the generalizability of the rules.
   - **Monitor for Overfitting:** A significant drop in performance metrics on the test data would indicate overfitting. Aim for a balance where both training and test metrics are satisfactory.

3. **Iterate on Rule Development:**
   - **Prioritize High-Impact Rules:** Focus on refining rules that have a substantial impact on both confidence and support.
   - **Eliminate Redundant or Low-Impact Rules:** Rules that contribute minimally to the predictive power or significantly overlap with others can be consolidated or removed.

4. **Expand Dataset if Possible:**
   - **Increase Data Diversity:** Introducing more diverse data may help in identifying patterns that are both accurate and widely applicable.
   - **Stratify Data Sampling:** Ensure that the training data represents all relevant segments of the population to avoid bias in rule learning.

5. **Leverage Domain Knowledge:**
   - **Consult Subject Matter Experts:** Use insights from experts to identify chemical structures or properties that are likely to generalize well.
   - **Incorporate Theoretical Foundations:** Align the rules with established chemical principles to enhance their plausibility and applicability.

6. **Optimize Rule Learning Parameters:**
   - **Adjust Support Thresholds:** Lowering the minimum support threshold slightly might allow more general rules to emerge without overwhelming the system with noise.
   - **Fine-Tune Confidence Requirements:** Setting an appropriate confidence threshold can help in balancing precision and recall.

**Conclusion:**

While the current rules display excellent confidence and lift on the training data, the low support indicates a need for further refinement. By focusing on increasing the rules' applicability without significantly sacrificing accuracy, the overall performance can be enhanced. Incorporating test metrics into the evaluation process and continuing iterative development will help in achieving a robust set of rules that generalize well and provide valuable insights into the catalytic problem being studied.

ML Commenter Message:
**Introduction**

Based on the latest training results and SHAP (SHapley Additive exPlanations) analysis, I have evaluated the performance of the current set of rules in predicting the loading of modifiers on catalysts for the catalytic problem at hand. The goal is to assess the effectiveness of the current rules, identify any issues related to overfitting or underfitting, and suggest improvements to enhance the model's predictive capabilities.

---

**Comments on Accuracies**

**Current Accuracy:**

- **Validation Accuracy:** An overall accuracy of **0.7429** was obtained using the classification model on the validation set.

**Reference Accuracies for Comparison:**

  - \[0.76, 0.76, 0.76, 0.76, 0.76, 0.76\]; **Test Accuracy:** 0.75
  - \[0.76, 0.68, 0.72, 0.76, 0.76, 0.76\]; **Test Accuracy:** 0.75

  - \[0.76, 0.76, 0.76, 0.76, 0.76, 0.76\]; **Test Accuracy:** 0.625

**Analysis:**

- The current validation accuracy of **74.29%** is slightly lower than the previous validation accuracies, which were consistently around **76%**.
- The decrease in accuracy suggests that the current set of rules does not significantly improve the model's performance compared to previous iterations.
- The consistency of previous accuracies indicates that the model might have reached a performance plateau, possibly a local minimum.
- The variation in previous test accuracies (ranging from **62.5%** to **75%**) suggests some instability in generalizing to new data.

**Overfitting/Underfitting Assessment:**

- The small decrease in validation accuracy implies that the model is **not severely overfitting** but may be **slightly underfitting**, as it fails to capture some patterns in the data.
- The consistent validation accuracies across folds indicate that the model's performance is stable but could benefit from refinement to improve generalization.

The SHAP values provide insights into how each rule contributes to the prediction for each molecule. Positive SHAP values indicate that the rule contributes to a prediction of **high loading**, while negative values contribute to **low loading**.

---

**Performance Evaluation of Current Rules**

**Effectiveness of Current Rules:**

- The current rules capture some of the key features influencing the loading of modifiers on catalysts.
- However, discrepancies between predicted and experimental values for certain molecules indicate that some rules may not generalize well to all structures.
- The model's performance plateau suggests that the current rule set may have limitations in capturing the complexity of the chemical space.

**Overfitting/Underfitting Assessment:**

- **Overfitting:** The model does not exhibit classic overfitting signs (high training accuracy but low validation accuracy). However, the validation accuracy is not improving, which may indicate that the model is too tightly fitted to the existing rules without capturing new patterns.
- **Underfitting:** The consistent but suboptimal performance suggests underfitting; the model may not be complex enough or the rules may not be sufficiently comprehensive to capture all relevant features.

**Local Minimum:**

- The model appears to have reached a local minimum in terms of accuracy, indicating that the current approach may have plateaued and requires new strategies to improve.

---

**Recommendations for Improvement**

Based on the SHAP analysis and performance evaluation, the following recommendations aim to refine the current rules and improve the model's predictive capabilities.

1. **Refine Rule 6:**

   - **Current Rule:** Modifiers containing multiple hydroxyl groups adjacent to the carboxylic acid group produce a high yield.
   - **Issue Identified:** Rule 6 contributes significantly to underpredicting the loading for molecules like *C1=CN=CC=C1CCCC(=O)O* and *C1=CC2=C(C=C1C(=O)O)C(=O)OC2=O*.
   - **Recommendation:**
     - **Broaden the Scope:** Adjust Rule 6 to consider the position and context of hydroxyl groups. For example, differentiate between aliphatic and aromatic systems.
     - **Incorporate Aromaticity:** Modify the rule to account for the effect of hydroxyl groups on aromatic rings, which may not enhance yield as in aliphatic cases.
     - **Update Rule 6:**
       - *Modifiers containing multiple hydroxyl groups adjacent to the carboxylic acid group in aliphatic chains produce high yield. In aromatic systems, the impact may vary and requires additional factors to be considered.*

2. **Adjust Rule 3:**

   - **Current Rule:** Benzoic acids substituted at the para position with strong electron-withdrawing groups result in high yield.
   - **Issue Identified:** Rule 3 negatively impacts molecules like *C1=CC2=C(C=C1C(=O)O)C(=O)OC2=O*, suggesting it may not account for certain electron-withdrawing groups in fused ring systems.
   - **Recommendation:**
     - **Expand to Fused Rings:** Include benzoic acids with fused aromatic systems and adjust for the electron-withdrawing effect of additional ring structures.
     - **Update Rule 3:**
       - *Benzoic acids substituted with strong electron-withdrawing groups, including in fused ring systems (e.g., benzofuranones), generally result in high yield, unless steric hindrance or resonance effects reduce their activity.*

3. **Revise Rule 2:**

   - **Current Rule:** Heteroaromatic carboxylic acids containing nitrogen atoms within a single ring lead to high yield.
   - **Issue Identified:** Molecules like *C1=CN=CC=C1CCCC(=O)O* are underpredicted, despite fitting Rule 2.
   - **Recommendation:**
     - **Consider Alkyl Chain Length:** The impact of the aliphatic linker length attached to the heteroaromatic ring may influence the yield.
     - **Incorporate Side Chains:**
       - Adjust Rule 2 to account for modifiers where the heteroaromatic ring is connected via long aliphatic chains.
     - **Update Rule 2:**
       - *Heteroaromatic carboxylic acids containing nitrogen atoms within a single ring lead to high yield, particularly when directly attached to the carboxylic acid group. If connected via long aliphatic chains, yield may decrease.*

4. **Reevaluate Rule 5:**

   - **Current Rule:** Aliphatic carboxylic acids with long, straight-chain alkyl groups (eight carbons or more) and no additional polar functional groups lead to high yield.
   - **Issue Identified:** *Octanoic acid* is correctly predicted, but *C8* acids like *CCCCCCCC(=O)O* are underpredicted.
   - **Recommendation:**
     - **Verify Data Accuracy:** Ensure that the data associated with these molecules is correct and that experimental values are accurate.
     - **Consider Chain Length Threshold:**
       - Perhaps the chain length threshold needs adjustment.
     - **Update Rule 5:**
       - *Aliphatic carboxylic acids with straight-chain alkyl groups of eight carbons may result in variable yields; chains longer than eight carbons reliably lead to high yield.*

5. **Integrate Additional Features:**

   - **Include Steric and Electronic Factors:**
     - Consider incorporating rules that account for steric hindrance and electronic effects not currently covered.
   - **Use Quantitative Descriptors:**
     - Utilize molecular descriptors such as LogP, polar surface area, or electronic properties to enhance rule definitions.

6. **Improve Generalization:**

   - **Simplify Overly Specific Rules:**
     - Merge similar rules to reduce complexity and avoid overfitting to specific molecules.
   - **Avoid Redundancies:**
     - Ensure that rules are mutually exclusive or clearly hierarchical to prevent conflicting contributions.

7. **Iterative Validation:**

   - **Test Refined Rules:**
     - Implement the adjusted rules and re-evaluate the model's performance.
   - **Monitor SHAP Contributions:**
     - Continuously assess SHAP values to identify remaining discrepancies and adjust accordingly.

---

**Conclusion**

The current set of rules provides a solid foundation for predicting the loading of modifiers on catalysts but requires refinement to improve accuracy and generalization. By adjusting the rules based on SHAP analysis insights—particularly focusing on Rules 2, 3, 5, and 6—we can enhance the model's ability to capture complex chemical interactions. Iterative testing and validation of these refined rules are essential to achieve improved predictive performance.

**Next Steps:**

- Implement the recommended rule adjustments.
- Retrain the model with the updated rules.
- Compare the new performance metrics to previous results.
- Continue the iterative process of analysis and refinement based on model performance and SHAP insights.

---

This approach aims to systematically enhance the predictive capabilities of the model by addressing specific shortcomings identified through detailed analysis. Collaboration and further discussion are encouraged to refine these recommendations and implement effective solutions.

Project Manager Message:
**Summary of Discussions and Directions for Optimization**

The current set of rules for predicting the catalytic performance in the remote δ-C(sp³)–H bond functionalization reaction has been thoroughly evaluated. While the rules show high confidence and lift on the training data, they have low support, indicating that they apply to a limited subset of the data. The validation accuracy has remained roughly constant, suggesting that the model may have reached a local minimum and is not capturing new, broader patterns in the data.

**Key Findings:**

- **High Confidence but Low Support:** The rules are precise but not widely applicable across the dataset.
- **Stable but Suboptimal Accuracy:** Validation accuracy is around 74.29%, slightly lower than previous iterations, indicating potential underfitting.
- **SHAP Analysis Insights:** Certain rules (notably Rules 2, 3, 5, and 6) contribute significantly to incorrect predictions, suggesting areas for refinement.
- **Possibility of Underfitting:** The model may not be complex enough to capture all relevant chemical features influencing the reaction yield.

**Suggestions to Improve Current Rules**

1. **Refine Rule 6 - Hydroxyl Groups:**
   - **Issue:** Rule 6 currently states that modifiers with multiple hydroxyl groups adjacent to the carboxylic acid group produce high yield. However, SHAP analysis shows that this rule negatively impacts predictions for certain molecules, especially those with hydroxyl groups on aromatic rings.
   - **Recommendation:** Modify Rule 6 to differentiate between aliphatic and aromatic systems.
     - **Updated Rule 6:** *Modifiers containing multiple hydroxyl groups adjacent to the carboxylic acid group in **aliphatic chains** tend to produce high yield, likely due to enhanced hydrogen bonding. In **aromatic systems**, the presence of hydroxyl groups may not have the same effect and can lead to varying yields based on their position and the overall electronic structure.*

2. **Adjust Rule 3 - Electron-Withdrawing Groups:**
   - **Issue:** Rule 3 focuses on benzoic acids substituted at the para position with strong electron-withdrawing groups leading to high yield. It does not account for fused ring systems where electronic effects can differ.
   - **Recommendation:** Expand Rule 3 to include fused aromatic systems and consider the impact of steric hindrance.
     - **Updated Rule 3:** *Modifiers that are benzoic acids or fused aromatic carboxylic acids substituted with strong electron-withdrawing groups (e.g., nitro, bromomethyl) tend to result in high yield. However, steric hindrance and resonance effects in fused ring systems may alter their reactivity and should be considered.*

3. **Revise Rule 2 - Heteroaromatic Carboxylic Acids:**
   - **Issue:** Rule 2 does not account for the impact of aliphatic linker length between the heteroaromatic ring and the carboxylic acid group.
   - **Recommendation:** Modify Rule 2 to consider the effect of linker length on yield.
     - **Updated Rule 2:** *Modifiers that are heteroaromatic carboxylic acids containing nitrogen atoms within a single ring lead to high yield when the carboxylic acid group is directly attached or connected via a short linker (one to two carbons). Longer aliphatic linkers may reduce this positive effect due to decreased electronic interaction.*

4. **Reevaluate Rule 5 - Aliphatic Chain Length:**
   - **Issue:** Rule 5 may not adequately capture the relationship between chain length and yield, as some long-chain acids are underpredicted.
   - **Recommendation:** Adjust the chain length threshold and consider the impact of chain length on solubility and steric effects.
     - **Updated Rule 5:** *Aliphatic carboxylic acids with long, straight-chain alkyl groups of **more than eight carbons** (nine or more) and no additional polar functional groups tend to lead to high yield, possibly due to favorable hydrophobic interactions. Acids with exactly eight carbons may have variable yields, and shorter chains generally result in lower yields.*

5. **Incorporate Additional Molecular Features:**
   - **Steric and Electronic Factors:** Introduce rules that account for steric hindrance, electronic effects, and resonance structures that can influence catalyst interactions.
   - **Quantitative Descriptors:** Utilize molecular descriptors such as LogP (lipophilicity), polar surface area, and electronic parameters to refine rules.

6. **Simplify and Generalize Rules:**
   - **Avoid Overly Specific Rules:** Combine similar rules or broaden them to cover more compounds without being too general.
   - **Hierarchy of Rules:** Establish a hierarchy where more general rules are applied first, followed by more specific ones, to improve prediction accuracy.

7. **Validate and Iterate:**
   - **Test Adjusted Rules:** Implement the refined rules and assess their performance on both training and validation datasets.
   - **Monitor for Overfitting:** Ensure that the changes lead to improved generalization and not just better performance on the training data.

**Possibility for Further Optimization**

Given the identified issues with specific rules and the potential for refining them to better capture the underlying chemical phenomena affecting yield, there is a clear opportunity for further optimization of the current rules without overfitting. By adjusting and enhancing the rules as suggested, we can aim to improve the model's accuracy and applicability to a wider range of compounds.

    **Start of Rules**

- **Rule 1**: Modifiers that are **α-amino acids with side chains containing carboxylic acid groups** (dicarboxylic amino acids) result in **High Yield**. Examples include aspartic acid and glutamic acid (e.g., *(2S)-2-aminobutanedioic acid*, *(2S)-2-aminopentanedioic acid*).

- **Rule 2**: Modifiers that are **heteroaromatic carboxylic acids containing nitrogen atoms within a single ring**, not fused to other rings, lead to **High Yield**. This includes compounds like pyridine and pyrazole carboxylic acids (e.g., *pyridine-3-carboxylic acid*, *1H-pyrazole-4-carboxylic acid*, *2-oxo-1H-pyridine-4-carboxylic acid*).

- **Rule 3**: Modifiers that are **benzoic acids substituted at the para position with strong electron-withdrawing groups** such as nitro or bromomethyl groups result in **High Yield** (e.g., *4-nitrobenzoic acid*, *4-(bromomethyl)benzoic acid*). In contrast, para-substitution with aldehyde groups leads to **Low Yield** (e.g., *4-formylbenzoic acid*).

- **Rule 4**: Modifiers that are **benzoic acids substituted at the para position with electron-donating methyl groups** result in **Low Yield** (e.g., *4-methylbenzoic acid*), whereas substitution with amino groups leads to **High Yield** (e.g., *4-aminobenzoic acid*). Therefore, the nature of the substituent significantly affects the yield.

- **Rule 5**: **Aliphatic carboxylic acids with long, straight-chain alkyl groups (eight carbons or more) and no additional polar functional groups** lead to **High Yield** (e.g., *octanoic acid*). The addition of polar groups like hydroxyl or amino groups at the terminal position reduces the yield, resulting in **Low Yield** (e.g., *7-hydroxyheptanoic acid*, *7-aminoheptanoic acid*, *8-aminooctanoic acid*).

- **Rule 6**: Modifiers containing **multiple hydroxyl groups adjacent to the carboxylic acid group** produce a **High Yield**, possibly due to increased hydrogen-bonding capabilities (e.g., *(2R,3S,4R,5R)-2,3,4,5,6-pentahydroxyhexanoic acid*).

- **Rule 7**: Modifiers with **perfluorinated alkyl chains** exhibit **Low Yield**, likely due to their strong electron-withdrawing effects and steric hindrance (e.g., *2,2,3,3,4,4,5,5,5-nonafluoropentanoic acid*).

- **Rule 8**: **Aliphatic thiol-containing modifiers** near the carboxylic acid group lead to **High Yield** (e.g., *3-sulfanylpropanoic acid*), whereas **aromatic thiol-containing modifiers** result in **Low Yield** (e.g., *4-sulfanylbenzoic acid*).

- **Rule 9**: **α-Amino acids with aromatic side chains** show different yields based on the aromatic group: **indole-containing side chains** (e.g., tryptophan) result in **High Yield** (*e.g., (2R)-2-amino-3-(1H-indol-3-yl)propanoic acid*), while **phenyl-containing side chains** (e.g., phenylalanine) lead to **Low Yield** (*e.g., (2S)-2-amino-3-phenylpropanoic acid*).

- **Rule 10**: Modifiers with **hydroxyphenyl groups connected via aliphatic chains longer than three carbons** to the carboxylic acid result in **High Yield** (e.g., *5-(4-hydroxyphenyl)pentanoic acid*). If the aliphatic chain is three carbons or shorter, the yield is **Low Yield** (e.g., *3-(4-hydroxyphenyl)propanoic acid*).

- **Rule 11**: Modifiers that are **aromatic carboxylic acids connected to heteroaromatic rings via an aliphatic linker** yield a **High Yield** (e.g., *4-pyridin-4-ylbutanoic acid*). If the aromatic rings are directly connected without an aliphatic linker, they result in **Low Yield** (e.g., *4-pyridin-4-ylbenzoic acid*).

- **Rule 12**: Modifiers containing **carboxylic acid groups attached to benzofuran or benzofuranone systems** promote **High Yield**, possibly due to their chelating abilities or extended π-conjugation (e.g., *1,3-dioxo-2-benzofuran-5-carboxylic acid*).

- **Rule 13**: Modifiers with **aldehyde and hydroxyl groups on adjacent positions of an aromatic ring** lead to **Low Yield**, potentially due to interactions that interfere with the catalyst (e.g., *5-formyl-2-hydroxybenzoic acid*).

- **Rule 14**: Modifiers with **polyether chains attached to carboxylic acids** result in **Low Yield**, likely due to steric hindrance and solubility issues (e.g., *2-[2-(2-methoxyethoxy)ethoxy]acetic acid*).

- **Rule 15**: Modifiers that are **peptides or contain amide bonds derived from amino acids with sulfur-containing side chains** lead to **Low Yield** (e.g., *2-[[(2S)-2-amino-4-methylsulfanylbutanoyl]amino]ethanoic acid*).

Rule Advisor Message:
** Start of Advice **

- **Advice 1**: **Refine Rule 6 to Differentiate Between Aliphatic and Aromatic Hydroxyl Groups**

  **Recommendation**: Update Rule 6 to specify that modifiers containing multiple hydroxyl groups adjacent to the carboxylic acid group in **aliphatic chains** tend to produce **High Yield** due to enhanced hydrogen bonding. For **aromatic systems**, the presence of hydroxyl groups may lead to varying yields depending on their position and the overall electronic structure.

  **Implementation**: Modify the SMILES-based rule to detect multiple hydroxyl groups attached to aliphatic carbons near the carboxylic acid group. For aromatic compounds, adjust the rule to consider the position of hydroxyl groups relative to the carboxylic acid and their electronic effects.

- **Advice 2**: **Adjust Rule 3 to Include Fused Aromatic Systems and Account for Steric Hindrance**

  **Recommendation**: Expand Rule 3 to include modifiers that are benzoic acids **or fused aromatic carboxylic acids** substituted with strong electron-withdrawing groups (e.g., nitro, bromomethyl). Consider steric hindrance and resonance effects in fused ring systems that may alter reactivity and impact yield.

  **Implementation**: Update the SMILES-based rule to identify fused aromatic systems with electron-withdrawing substituents at key positions. Incorporate steric factors by evaluating the size and location of substituents that might hinder catalyst interaction.

- **Advice 3**: **Revise Rule 2 to Account for Aliphatic Linker Length in Heteroaromatic Carboxylic Acids**

  **Recommendation**: Modify Rule 2 to state that modifiers which are heteroaromatic carboxylic acids containing nitrogen atoms within a single ring lead to **High Yield** when the carboxylic acid group is directly attached or connected via a short aliphatic linker (one to two carbons). Longer aliphatic linkers may reduce the positive effect due to decreased electronic interaction.

  **Implementation**: Adjust the SMILES-based rule to detect heteroaromatic rings with nitrogen atoms that are directly connected or have short linkers to the carboxylic acid group. Exclude compounds with longer linkers from this rule.

- **Advice 4**: **Reevaluate Rule 5 by Adjusting Aliphatic Chain Length Criteria**

  **Recommendation**: Update Rule 5 to reflect that aliphatic carboxylic acids with long, straight-chain alkyl groups of **more than eight carbons** (nine or more) and no additional polar functional groups tend to lead to **High Yield**. Acids with exactly eight carbons may have variable yields, and shorter chains generally result in **Low Yield**.

  **Implementation**: Modify the SMILES-based rule to specify chain lengths greater than eight carbons (C9 and longer) when identifying high-yield modifiers. Reclassify modifiers with eight or fewer carbons accordingly.

- **Advice 5**: **Incorporate Steric and Electronic Factors into Existing and New Rules**

  **Recommendation**: Introduce rules that account for steric hindrance, electronic effects, and resonance structures affecting catalyst interactions. Utilize molecular descriptors like LogP, polar surface area, and electronic parameters to refine predictions.

  **Implementation**: Enhance SMILES-based rules by including substructure searches for bulky groups or branching that may cause steric hindrance. Incorporate electron-withdrawing or donating characteristics of substituents to assess their impact on yield.

- **Advice 6**: **Simplify and Generalize Rules to Improve Applicability**

  **Recommendation**: Avoid overly specific rules by combining similar ones and broadening criteria where appropriate. Establish a hierarchy where general rules are applied first, followed by more specific ones, to enhance prediction accuracy.

  **Implementation**: Review existing rules for overlap and redundancy. Merge rules with common features, such as those involving electron-withdrawing groups on aromatic systems. Prioritize rule application from general to specific in the rule set.

- **Advice 7**: **Implement and Validate Updated Rules with Iterative Testing**

  **Recommendation**: After updating the rules as per the above advice, implement them and assess their performance on both training and validation datasets. Monitor for improvements in accuracy and adjust as necessary to prevent overfitting.

  **Implementation**: Apply the refined rules to the datasets and evaluate prediction accuracy. Use cross-validation techniques to ensure the rules generalize well. Refine the rules iteratively based on performance metrics.

** End of Advice **

Introduction of **terminal polar groups** like hydroxyl or amino groups reduces the yield, resulting in **Low Yield** (e.g., *7-hydroxyheptanoic acid*, *7-aminoheptanoic acid*, *8-aminooctanoic acid*).

- **Rule 6**: Modifiers with **perfluorinated alkyl chains** exhibit **Low Yield**, likely due to their strong electron-withdrawing effects and steric hindrance (e.g., *2,2,3,3,4,4,5,5,5-nonafluoropentanoic acid*).

For example, tryptophan with an indole ring results in high yield (*e.g., (2R)-2-amino-3-(1H-indol-3-yl)propanoic acid*), while phenylalanine with a phenyl ring leads to **Low Yield** (*e.g., (2S)-2-amino-3-phenylpropanoic acid*).

If the aliphatic chain is three carbons or shorter, the yield is **Low** (e.g., *3-(4-hydroxyphenyl)propanoic acid*).

If the aromatic rings are directly connected without an aliphatic linker, they lead to **Low Yield** (e.g., *4-pyridin-4-ylbenzoic acid*).

- **Rule 11**: Modifiers containing **carboxylic acid groups attached to benzofuran or benzofuranone systems** promote **High Yield**, possibly due to their chelating abilities or extended π-conjugation (e.g., *1,3-dioxo-2-benzofuran-5-carboxylic acid*).

- **Rule 12**: Modifiers with **aldehyde and hydroxyl groups on adjacent positions of an aromatic ring** lead to **Low Yield**, potentially due to intramolecular interactions that interfere with catalyst coordination (e.g., *5-formyl-2-hydroxybenzoic acid*).

- **Rule 13**: Modifiers with **polyether chains attached to carboxylic acids** result in **Low Yield**, likely due to steric hindrance and solubility issues (e.g., *2-[2-(2-methoxyethoxy)ethoxy]acetic acid*).

- **Rule 14**: Modifiers that are **peptides or contain amide bonds derived from amino acids with sulfur-containing side chains** lead to **Low Yield** (e.g., *2-[[(2S)-2-amino-4-methylsulfanylbutanoyl]amino]ethanoic acid*).

- **Rule 15**: Modifiers with **functional groups capable of coordinating to metal centers (e.g., amino, hydroxyl, carboxyl groups) positioned to facilitate chelation** tend to result in **High Yield**. The spatial arrangement of these groups enhances interaction with the catalyst (e.g., *4-aminobenzoic acid*).

Rule Commenter Message:
**Start of Comments**
- **Comment 1**: Rule 1 is clear and specifies that α-amino acids with side chains containing carboxylic acid groups result in High Yield. This rule is supported by data points 0 and 2 ((2S)-2-aminopentanedioic acid and (2S)-2-aminobutanedioic acid), both showing High Yield. The rule offers good physicochemical insight, suggesting that additional carboxylic acid groups enhance interactions with the catalyst. The complexity is appropriate, considering the combination of functional groups, and the coverage is adequate with at least two data points supporting the rule.

- **Comment 2**: Rule 2 clearly states that heteroaromatic carboxylic acids containing nitrogen atoms within a single, non-fused ring lead to High Yield. Supported by data points 7, 15, and 21 (2-oxo-1H-pyridine-4-carboxylic acid, 1H-pyrazole-4-carboxylic acid, and pyridine-3-carboxylic acid), all showing High Yield, the rule provides solid physicochemical insight into the role of nitrogen heterocycles in catalyst coordination. The complexity is suitable, and the coverage is strong with multiple data points.

- **Comment 3**: Rule 3 is clear about the effect of para-substituents on benzoic acids: electron-withdrawing groups lead to High Yield, while electron-donating groups result in Low Yield. This is supported by data points 1 and 20 (4-(bromomethyl)benzoic acid and 4-nitrobenzoic acid) showing High Yield, and data points 19 and 22 (4-formylbenzoic acid and 4-methylbenzoic acid) showing Low Yield. The rule provides good physicochemical insight into electronic effects on reactivity. The complexity and coverage are appropriate. However, data point 3 (4-aminobenzoic acid) is an exception, exhibiting High Yield despite having an electron-donating amino group, suggesting the rule may need refinement to account for such exceptions.

- **Comment 4**: Rule 4 clearly indicates that long-chain aliphatic carboxylic acids without additional polar groups result in High Yield, supported by data point 4 (octanoic acid). Modifiers with terminal polar groups yield Low Yield, as seen in data points 29, 31, and 34 (7-hydroxyheptanoic acid, 7-aminoheptanoic acid, and 8-aminooctanoic acid). The rule provides physicochemical insight into how polar groups affect catalyst interaction. The complexity is appropriate, and the coverage is satisfactory with multiple data points.

- **Comment 5**: Rule 5 states that modifiers with multiple hydroxyl groups adjacent to the carboxylic acid group produce High Yield. ML Commenter Message:
**Evaluation of Current Rules Effectiveness**

The current set of rules appears to be less effective than in previous iterations. The latest 5-fold cross-validation accuracy is **71.43%**, a decrease from the consistent **76%** observed in prior validations. This drop suggests that the current rules may not be capturing all the relevant features necessary for accurate predictions.

**Assessment of Overfitting/Underfitting**

The decrease in validation accuracy, coupled with fluctuating test accuracies in previous iterations (ranging from **62.5%** to **75%**), indicates potential issues with model fitting:

- **Underfitting**: The model may be too simplistic, failing to capture the complexity of the data. This is suggested by the consistent validation accuracies in previous iterations and the decrease in the current iteration.
- **Overfitting**: If certain rules are too specifically tailored to the training data, they may not generalize well to new data, leading to decreased performance.

**Analysis of Discrepancies Using SHAP Values**

To identify problematic rules, we examine molecules where the predicted loading does not match the experimental value and analyze the SHAP values to pinpoint the contributing rules.

---

**1. Molecules with Low Predicted Loading but High Experimental Value**

a. **Molecule**: C([C@H]([C@H]([C@@H]([C@H](C(=O)O)O)O)O)O)O (Glucose)

- **Prediction**: Low Loading
- **Experimental Value**: High Loading
- **Significant Negative SHAP Values**:

  - **Rule 2**: -0.08108
  - **Rule 8**: -0.03264
  - **Rule 9**: -0.02913

- **Implication**: **Rule 2** is contributing significantly to the incorrect low prediction.

b. **Molecule**: C1=CN=CC=C1CCCC(=O)O

- **Prediction**: Low Loading
- **Experimental Value**: High Loading
- **Significant Negative SHAP Values**:

  - **Rule 2**: -0.08108
  - **Rules 8 & 9**: Similar negative contributions

- **Implication**: Again, **Rule 2** is contributing negatively.

c. **Molecule**: C1=CC2=C(C=C1C(=O)O)C(=O)OC2=O

- **Prediction**: Low Loading
- **Experimental Value**: High Loading
- **Significant Negative SHAP Values**:

  - **Rule 2**: -0.08802
  - **Rule 3**: -0.08202

- **Implication**: Both **Rule 2** and **Rule 3** are contributing to incorrect predictions.

d. **Molecule**: C(CS)C(=O)O

- **Prediction**: Low Loading
- **Experimental Value**: High Loading
- **Significant Negative SHAP Values**:

  - **Rule 2**: -0.08802
  - **Rule 3**: -0.08202

- **Implication**: Similar pattern with **Rule 2**.

---

**2. Molecules with High Predicted Loading but Low Experimental Value**

No specific molecules in the provided data fit this category, suggesting that overestimation is less of an issue in this iteration.

---

**Interpretation of Accuracy Improvements**

The decrease in validation accuracy from **76%** to **71.43%** indicates that the current rules may have led to a reduction in model performance. The metrics suggest that the probability of improvement using the current set of rules is low unless adjustments are made.

**Deriving Insights from SHAP Analysis**

- **Rule 2**: Consistently has highly negative SHAP values for molecules where the predicted loading is low but should be high. This suggests that **Rule 2** may be too restrictive or mischaracterizing certain modifiers.
  
- **Rule 3 and Rule 8**: Also show significant negative SHAP values in some misclassifications, indicating potential areas for refinement.

**Performance Evaluation of Current Rules**

- **Effectiveness**: The current rules are less effective compared to previous iterations, as evidenced by the decrease in accuracy.
- **Overfitting/Underfitting**: The model may be underfitting, failing to capture the necessary complexity to make accurate predictions.
- **Local Minimum**: The consistent validation accuracies in previous iterations and lack of improvement suggest that a local minimum may have been reached.

**Recommendations for Improvement**

1. **Refine Rule 2**

   - **Current Rule 2**: *Modifiers that are heteroaromatic carboxylic acids containing nitrogen atoms within a single, non-fused ring lead to High Yield.*
   - **Issue**: This rule negatively impacts molecules like glucose, which is not a heteroaromatic carboxylic acid but is being misclassified due to Rule 2's influence.
   - **Recommendation**:

     - **Specificity**: Modify Rule 2 to be more specific, ensuring it targets only the intended compounds.
     - **Exclusions**: Introduce exceptions for molecules that do not fit the rule's intent but are being affected.
     - **Revised Rule 2**: *Modifiers that are heteroaromatic carboxylic acids containing nitrogen atoms within a single, non-fused ring lead to High Yield, **excluding compounds with multiple hydroxyl groups or aliphatic chains exceeding three carbons**.*

2. **Adjust Rule 3**

   - **Issue**: Rule 3 is contributing to misclassification in certain molecules with negative SHAP values.
   - **Recommendation**:

     - **Re-evaluation**: Reassess the criteria for electron-withdrawing and electron-donating groups.
     - **Sub-Rules**: Consider splitting Rule 3 into sub-rules to account for different substituents' effects more accurately.

3. **Modify Rule 8**

   - **Current Rule 8**: *α-Amino acids with side chains capable of forming hydrogen bonds or π-stacking interactions lead to High Yield.*
   - **Issue**: Negative SHAP values indicate misclassification for some molecules.
   - **Recommendation**:

     - **Clarification**: Define the specific side chains and interactions that lead to High Yield.
     - **Adjustment**: Exclude certain functional groups that may not contribute positively as initially thought.

4. **Introduce New Rules or Features**

   - **Observation**: Some molecules with high experimental loading are not being predicted correctly, possibly due to missing features.
   - **Recommendation**:

     - **Rule Addition**: Identify common structural features in misclassified high-loading molecules and create new rules to capture these.
     - **Example**: If sugars like glucose are misclassified, a new rule recognizing polyhydroxylated aliphatic carboxylic acids might improve accuracy.

5. **Enhance Generalization**

   - **Issue**: The model may be underfitting due to overly generalized or overly specific rules.
   - **Recommendation**:

     - **Balance Specificity**: Ensure rules are neither too broad nor too narrow.
     - **Data Augmentation**: Incorporate more diverse training data to improve generalization.
     - **Feature Engineering**: Introduce additional features that capture other molecular properties influencing yield (e.g., molecular weight, solubility).

6. **Iterative Refinement**

   - **Process**:

     - **Re-train**: Update the feature matrix with revised rules and re-train the model.
     - **Validate**: Perform cross-validation to assess improvements.
     - **SHAP Analysis**: Continuously use SHAP values to monitor the impact of rules and adjust accordingly.

7. **Review Rules with High SHAP Values**

   - **Positive SHAP Values in Overestimations**: For any molecules where the predicted loading is high but experimental value is low (not present in current data), rules with high positive SHAP values would need adjustment.
   - **Negative SHAP Values in Underestimations**: Focus on rules with high negative SHAP values in underestimations (e.g., Rule 2 in current data).

8. **Leverage Domain Knowledge**

   - **Chemistry Expertise**: Consult with chemists to understand underlying chemical principles that may not be captured by current rules.
   - **Mechanistic Insights**: Incorporate mechanistic understanding of catalyst-modifier interactions to inform rule adjustments.

**Conclusion**

The current rules require refinement to improve model performance. By focusing on problematic rules identified through SHAP analysis—particularly **Rule 2**, **Rule 3**, and **Rule 8**—and by enhancing the model's ability to generalize, we can work towards increasing the accuracy of predictions. Iterative testing and validation, coupled with domain expertise, will be essential in developing a more robust set of rules for predicting catalyst modifier loadings.

---

**Next Steps**

- **Revise the identified rules** based on the recommendations.
- **Update the feature matrix** accordingly.
- **Retrain the model** and monitor performance metrics.
- **Perform additional SHAP analyses** to assess the impact of changes.
- **Repeat the process** until satisfactory performance is achieved.

By systematically addressing the weaknesses highlighted, we can enhance the predictive capabilities of the model and improve the outcomes of the catalytic problem research program.

Project Manager Message:
**Summary of Discussions and Directions for Optimization:**

The current set of rules developed for predicting the yield of the remote δ-C(sp³)–H bond functionalization reaction shows a significant improvement in confidence and lift compared to previous iterations. The training confidence has increased to **94.92%**, and the lift has nearly doubled to **1.8987**, indicating that the rules are more precise and have stronger predictive power when their conditions are met.

However, the support for the current rules has decreased to **6.48%**, suggesting that while the rules are more accurate when applicable, they cover a smaller portion of the dataset. Additionally, the cross-validation accuracy has decreased from **76%** in previous iterations to **71.43%** in the current iteration. This drop in validation accuracy, along with insights from SHAP analysis, indicates that some rules may not be capturing all relevant features or may be misclassifying certain compounds.

**Suggestions for Improving Current Rules:**

1. **Review and Refine Rule 2:**
   - **Issue:** Rule 2 is contributing significantly to incorrect low-yield predictions for molecules that actually exhibit high experimental yields. SHAP analysis shows large negative contributions from Rule 2 in these misclassifications.
   - **Action:** 
     - **Specify Criteria:** Refine Rule 2 to be more specific about the types of heteroaromatic carboxylic acids it includes. Ensure that it accurately targets compounds with nitrogen-containing heteroaromatic rings without inadvertently applying to unrelated molecules.
     - **Introduce Exceptions:** Add exceptions for molecules with multiple hydroxyl groups or long aliphatic chains that do not fit the intended scope of Rule 2.

2. **Adjust Rule 3:**
   - **Issue:** Rule 3 has significant negative SHAP values in some misclassifications, suggesting it may be too broad or misapplied in certain contexts.
   - **Action:** 
     - **Re-evaluate Substituent Effects:** Re-express Rule 3 to account for the varying impact of different substituents. Differentiate between strong and moderate electron-withdrawing/donating groups.
     - **Split into Sub-Rules:** Consider dividing Rule 3 into more granular sub-rules that address specific substituents individually, improving precision in predictions.

3. **Modify Rule 8:**
   - **Issue:** Rule 8 shows negative SHAP contributions in some cases, indicating that it may not be accurately capturing the influence of side-chain interactions.
   - **Action:** 
     - **Clarify Side-Chain Characteristics:** Specify which side chains are beneficial for high yield, focusing on particular functional groups or structural motifs.
     - **Exclude Non-Contributing Groups:** Identify and exclude side chains that do not enhance yield or may even hinder it, refining the rule's applicability.

4. **Introduce New Rules or Features:**
   - **Observation:** Certain high-yielding molecules are not being predicted accurately, possibly due to missing structural features in the current rules.
   - **Action:** 
     - **Analyze Misclassified Molecules:** Identify common features among misclassified high-yield modifiers, such as polyhydroxylation or specific aliphatic chain lengths.
     - **Develop New Rules:** Create additional rules that capture these features, ensuring that structurally similar compounds are correctly classified.

5. **Balance Precision and Coverage:**
   - **Issue:** The current rules have high confidence and lift but low support, limiting their practical applicability.
   - **Action:**
     - **Broaden Rule Conditions:** Slightly relax overly strict rules to encompass a broader range of compounds without substantially sacrificing confidence.
     - **Optimize Support:** Aim to increase the percentage of the dataset covered by the rules to enhance their usefulness.

6. **Analyze Misclassified Instances with SHAP Values:**
   - **Action:**
     - **Identify Influential Rules:** Use SHAP analysis to pinpoint which rules contribute most to incorrect predictions.
     - **Adjust or Exclude Problematic Rules:** Modify or remove rules that consistently lead to misclassifications.

7. **Enhance Generalization:**
   - **Issue:** Overly specialized rules may lead to underfitting or overfitting.
   - **Action:**
     - **Ensure Rule Generality:** Maintain a balance between specificity and generality in rule formulation.
     - **Incorporate Additional Features:** Include other relevant chemical descriptors, such as molecular weight, hydrophobicity, or steric factors, to improve model robustness.

8. **Iterative Rule Refinement and Validation:**
   - **Process:**
     - **Update Feature Matrix:** Incorporate revised rules into the feature matrix.
     - **Retrain Model:** Re-train the model using the updated feature set.
     - **Cross-Validation:** Perform cross-validation to assess improvements in accuracy and avoid overfitting.
     - **Repeat as Necessary:** Continue the iterative process until satisfactory performance is achieved.

Yes, there is potential for further optimization of the current rules without overfitting. Adjusting the balance between precision and coverage will also improve the applicability and generalizability of the rules.

    **Start of Rules**

- **Rule 1**: Modifiers that are **α-amino acids with side chains containing carboxylic acid groups** (dicarboxylic amino acids) result in **High Yield**. Examples include aspartic acid and glutamic acid (e.g., *(2S)-2-aminobutanedioic acid*, *(2S)-2-aminopentanedioic acid*).

- **Rule 2**: Modifiers that are **heteroaromatic carboxylic acids containing nitrogen atoms within a single, non-fused ring** lead to **High Yield**. This includes compounds like pyridine and pyrazole carboxylic acids (e.g., *pyridine-3-carboxylic acid*, *1H-pyrazole-4-carboxylic acid*, *2-oxo-1H-pyridine-4-carboxylic acid*).

- **Rule 3**: Modifiers that are **para-substituted benzoic acids with strongly electron-withdrawing groups** (e.g., nitro, bromomethyl) result in **High Yield** (e.g., *4-nitrobenzoic acid*, *4-(bromomethyl)benzoic acid*). In contrast, para-substitution with electron-donating groups or carbonyl groups leads to **Low Yield** (e.g., *4-methylbenzoic acid*, *4-formylbenzoic acid*).

- **Rule 4**: Modifiers that are **long-chain aliphatic carboxylic acids (seven carbons or more) without additional polar functional groups** lead to **High Yield** (e.g., *octanoic acid*). Introduction of **terminal polar groups** like hydroxyl or amino groups reduces the yield, resulting in **Low Yield** (e.g., *7-hydroxyheptanoic acid*, *7-aminoheptanoic acid*, *8-aminooctanoic acid*).

- **Rule 5**: Modifiers containing **multiple hydroxyl groups adjacent to the carboxylic acid group** (sugar acids) produce a **High Yield**, possibly due to increased hydrogen-bonding capabilities (e.g., *(2R,3S,4R,5R)-2,3,4,5,6-pentahydroxyhexanoic acid*).

- **Rule 6**: Modifiers with **perfluorinated alkyl chains** exhibit **Low Yield**, likely due to their strong electron-withdrawing effects and steric hindrance (e.g., *2,2,3,3,4,4,5,5,5-nonafluoropentanoic acid*).

- **Rule 7**: Modifiers with **thiol groups near the carboxylic acid** produce **High Yield** when the thiol is on an aliphatic chain (e.g., *3-sulfanylpropanoic acid*), but **Low Yield** when the thiol is on an aromatic ring (e.g., *4-sulfanylbenzoic acid*).

- **Rule 8**: **α-Amino acids with side chains capable of forming hydrogen bonds or π-stacking interactions** lead to **High Yield**. For example, tryptophan with an indole ring results in high yield (*e.g., (2R)-2-amino-3-(1H-indol-3-yl)propanoic acid*), while phenylalanine with a phenyl ring leads to **Low Yield** (*e.g., (2S)-2-amino-3-phenylpropanoic acid*).

- **Rule 9**: Modifiers with **hydroxyphenyl groups connected via aliphatic chains longer than three carbons** to the carboxylic acid result in **High Yield** (e.g., *5-(4-hydroxyphenyl)pentanoic acid*). If the aliphatic chain is three carbons or shorter, the yield is **Low** (e.g., *3-(4-hydroxyphenyl)propanoic acid*).

- **Rule 10**: Modifiers that are **aromatic carboxylic acids connected to heteroaromatic rings via an aliphatic linker** result in **High Yield** (e.g., *4-pyridin-4-ylbutanoic acid*). If the aromatic rings are directly connected without an aliphatic linker, they lead to **Low Yield** (e.g., *4-pyridin-4-ylbenzoic acid*).

- **Rule 11**: Modifiers containing **carboxylic acid groups attached to benzofuran or benzofuranone systems** promote **High Yield**, possibly due to their chelating abilities or extended π-conjugation (e.g., *1,3-dioxo-2-benzofuran-5-carboxylic acid*).

- **Rule 12**: Modifiers with **aldehyde and hydroxyl groups on adjacent positions of an aromatic ring** lead to **Low Yield**, potentially due to intramolecular interactions that interfere with catalyst coordination (e.g., *5-formyl-2-hydroxybenzoic acid*).

- **Rule 13**: Modifiers with **polyether chains attached to carboxylic acids** result in **Low Yield**, likely due to steric hindrance and solubility issues (e.g., *2-[2-(2-methoxyethoxy)ethoxy]acetic acid*).

- **Rule 14**: Modifiers that are **peptides or contain amide bonds derived from amino acids with sulfur-containing side chains** lead to **Low Yield** (e.g., *2-[[(2S)-2-amino-4-methylsulfanylbutanoyl]amino]ethanoic acid*).

- **Rule 15**: Modifiers with **functional groups capable of coordinating to metal centers (e.g., amino, hydroxyl, carboxyl groups) positioned to facilitate chelation** tend to result in **High Yield**. The spatial arrangement of these groups enhances interaction with the catalyst (e.g., *4-aminobenzoic acid*).

    - **Implementation**: Update the SMILES patterns in Rule 2 to precisely match the intended heteroaromatic rings, for example:

      - Exclude patterns that do not match the specified heteroaromatic rings.

- **Advice 2**: **Adjust Rule 3 to Account for Substituent Effects More Precisely**

  - **Issue**: Rule 3's broad categorization of strong electron-withdrawing groups leads to misclassifications.
  
  - **Action**:
    - **Differentiate Substituents**: Re-express Rule 3 to distinguish between different electron-withdrawing groups based on their strength (e.g., nitro vs. halogens).
    - **Split Rule 3 into Sub-Rules**:
      - **Rule 3a**: Para-substituted benzoic acids with very strong electron-withdrawing groups (nitro, trifluoromethyl) result in **High Yield**.
      - **Rule 3b**: Para-substituted benzoic acids with moderately electron-withdrawing groups (halogens like Br, Cl) may not guarantee high yield and should be evaluated individually.
    - **Implementation**: Use specific SMILES patterns to identify substituents:
      - For Rule 3a: `O=C(O)c1ccc(cc1)[N+](=O)[O-]` (4-nitrobenzoic acid).
      - For Rule 3b: Adjust patterns to include or exclude specific halogen substitutions.

**Start of Comments**

- **Comment 1**: Rule 1 is clear in specifying that α-amino acids containing two carboxylic acid groups (dicarboxylic amino acids) result in High Yield. The rule is supported by at least two data points—(2S)-2-aminobutanedioic acid and (2S)-2-aminopentanedioic acid—both yielding high. The physical-chemical insight is strong, as the additional carboxylic acid group can enhance coordination with the catalytic metal center. The rule effectively balances complexity and coverage by considering a combination of functional groups with adequate supporting data.

- **Comment 2**: Rule 2 states that aromatic carboxylic acids with electron-withdrawing substituents at the para position lead to High Yield, while those with electron-donating substituents or aldehyde groups result in Low Yield. While supported by data for 4-nitrobenzoic acid and 4-(bromomethyl)benzoic acid (High Yield) and 4-methylbenzoic acid and 4-formylbenzoic acid (Low Yield), the rule is contradicted by 4-aminobenzoic acid, which has an electron-donating amino group but results in High Yield. This inconsistency reduces clarity and suggests the rule may not fully capture the underlying chemical behavior. The property insight is moderate, and coverage is acceptable but undermined by contradictory data.

- **Comment 3**: Rule 3 is clear in defining that heteroaromatic carboxylic acids with nitrogen atoms in a single non-fused ring yield High Yield, whereas compounds with fused rings or additional aromatic rings may yield Low Yield. This rule is supported by pyridine-3-carboxylic acid and 1H-pyrazole-4-carboxylic acid (both High Yield), and 4-pyridin-4-ylbenzoic acid (Low Yield). The property insight is solid, as the nitrogen heteroatoms can coordinate with the metal catalyst. The rule exhibits appropriate complexity and has satisfactory coverage and balance.

- **Comment 4**: Rule 4 states that aliphatic carboxylic acids with long hydrocarbon chains (C7 and above) without polar terminal groups result in High Yield, whereas the introduction of polar terminal groups decreases yield. Supported by octanoic acid (High Yield) and 7-hydroxyheptanoic acid and 7-aminoheptanoic acid (both Low Yield), the rule is clear and provides good property insight into how chain length and polarity affect catalyst interaction. The complexity is appropriate, and coverage is strong with multiple supporting data points.

- **Comment 5**: Rule 5 suggests that α-amino acids with aromatic side chains capable of π-stacking interactions result in Low Yield, but those with additional hydrogen-bonding functional groups in the side chain lead to High Yield. The rule is clear, provides strong property insight into the spatial effects of thiol groups, and has satisfactory coverage and complexity.

Supported by 4-pyridin-4-ylbutanoic acid (High Yield) and 4-pyridin-4-ylbenzoic acid (Low Yield), the rule is clear and insightful regarding the importance of molecular flexibility and distance between functional groups. The complexity and coverage are appropriate, making it a strong rule.

Supported by 2,2,3,3,4,4,5,5,5-nonafluoropentanoic acid (Low Yield), the rule is clear and offers good property insight. - **Comment 11**: Rule 11 states that modifiers with aldehyde and hydroxyl groups on adjacent positions of an aromatic ring lead to Low Yield. Supported by 5-formyl-2-hydroxybenzoic acid (Low Yield), the rule is clear and provides property insight into potential interference with catalyst interaction. However, the rule is based on a single data point, resulting in limited coverage.

- **Comment 12**: Rule 12 proposes that carboxylic acids with extended conjugation or fused aromatic systems can lead to High Yield due to enhanced π-interactions. While supported by 1,3-dioxo-2-benzofuran-5-carboxylic acid (High Yield), the rule lacks clarity due to its broad definition and has limited coverage with only one supporting data point.

- **Comment 13**: Rule 13 suggests that simple aliphatic carboxylic acids without additional functional groups shorter than C7 result in High Yield, but the example provided is octanoic acid (C8), which contradicts the rule. This inconsistency reduces clarity and indicates possible errors in the rule's formulation. Coverage is unclear due to this contradiction, and the rule's effectiveness is compromised.

- **Comment 14**: Rule 14 states that peptides or compounds containing amide bonds derived from amino acids with sulfur-containing side chains lead to Low Yield. Supported by 2-[[(2S)-2-amino-4-methylsulfanylbutanoyl]amino]ethanoic acid (Low Yield), the rule is clear and provides property insight. However, coverage is limited with only one supporting data point, affecting its overall strength.

- **Comment 15**: Rule 15 suggests that modifiers possessing functional groups capable of chelating the metal center result in High Yield. While 4-aminobenzoic acid (High Yield) supports the rule, 2-[(4-hydroxybenzoyl)amino]acetic acid (Low Yield) contradicts it, as it contains potential chelating groups but results in Low Yield. This inconsistency affects clarity and indicates that the rule may not consistently predict outcomes. Coverage is limited and undermined by conflicting data.

**End of Comments**

---

**Recommendations for Improving Current Rules**

While the current rules perform well, there is room for refinement to enhance their applicability and robustness.

**1. **Rule Consolidation and Simplification****

- **Merge Overlapping Rules:**
  - Identify and combine rules with similar chemical features to reduce redundancy.
  - For example, **Rules 1, 5, and 14** all involve amino acids with specific side chains; consider integrating these into a more general rule with subcategories.

**2. **Enhance Specificity and Coverage****

- **Address Edge Cases:**
  - Examine instances where the rules may not accurately predict yields.
  - Modify rules to account for exceptions, ensuring they cover more data points without sacrificing confidence.

- **Incorporate Structural Descriptors:**
  - Include additional molecular descriptors such as steric hindrance, electron density, or hydrogen bonding capacity to improve rule specificity.

**3. **Balance Between Support and Confidence****

- **Increase Support Without Losing Predictive Power:**
  - Aim to slightly broaden the rules to increase their applicability (support) while maintaining high confidence levels.
  - This can be achieved by adjusting the criteria in the rules to encompass more compounds.

**4. **Validation with External Data****

- **Test on Validation Set:**
  - Apply the current rules to a separate validation dataset to assess their predictive power outside the training data.
  - This step is crucial to ensure that the high confidence and lift are not merely due to overfitting.

**5. **Monitor for Overfitting****

- **Regularization Techniques:**
  - Implement techniques to prevent overfitting, such as cross-validation or pruning rules that are too narrowly defined.

- **Review Unusually High Metrics:**
  - Extremely high confidence and lift values can be a red flag for overfitting.
  - Ensure that the rules are based on meaningful chemical relationships rather than anomalies in the data.

**6. **Chemical Relevance and Mechanistic Insight****

- **Expert Review:**
  - Have chemists review the rules to confirm that they make sense mechanistically.
  - For example, assess whether the chemical rationale behind high or low yields aligns with known catalytic behaviors.

- **Incorporate Mechanistic Understanding:**
  - Enhance rules by integrating known reaction mechanisms and catalyst-modifier interactions.
  - This will improve the rules' robustness and applicability to related systems.

**7. **Iterative Refinement****

- **Feedback Loop:**
  - Use insights from validation and expert review to refine the rules iteratively.
  - Continually update the rules with new data as it becomes available to keep them current.

---

**Conclusion**

The current set of rules demonstrates a significant improvement in both confidence and lift, indicating strong predictive power for the catalytic problem in question. The high confidence values suggest that the rules are reliable, while the elevated lift values confirm that the associations are meaningful and not due to random chance.

The stabilization of metrics across iterations suggests that a local minimum has been reached. Therefore, it appears that sufficient iterations have been conducted for this phase of the model development.

To further improve the rules:

- **Broaden Rule Applicability:** Slightly expand the conditions to cover more data while maintaining high confidence.
- **Prevent Overfitting:** Validate the rules with external data and adjust to ensure they are generalizable.
- **Enhance Chemical Validity:** Incorporate mechanistic insights and expert feedback to ensure the rules make chemical sense.
- **Continuous Improvement:** Establish an iterative process for ongoing refinement as new data and insights become available.

By focusing on these areas, the current rules can be improved to enhance their effectiveness and reliability in predicting catalytic yields, ultimately advancing the research program's objectives.
