Project Manager Message:
**Summary and Suggestions for Optimizing Current Rules**

The current set of rules for predicting the yield of the radical-mediated remote δ-C(sp³)–H bond functionalization reaction shows improved confidence and lift compared to previous iterations. However, there are areas for further optimization to enhance model performance and accuracy.

**Key Points from the Discussion:**

1. **Performance Metrics:**
   - **High Confidence and Lift:** The current rules have significantly improved confidence (approximately 94.5%) and lift (average of 1.89), indicating strong predictive power when the rule conditions are met.
   - **Lower Support:** The support for the rules is low (average of 6.86%), meaning the rules apply to a smaller portion of the dataset. Increasing support without sacrificing confidence is desirable.

2. **Validation Accuracy:**
   - The current cross-validation accuracy is **77.14%**, which is an improvement but not the highest observed. There is potential to achieve higher accuracy with rule refinement.

3. **SHAP Analysis Insights:**
   - The SHAP values highlight that certain rules contribute to misclassifications. Specifically, **Rule 2, Rule 3, Rule 4, and Rule 10** either overestimate or underestimate the yield in some molecules.
   - Overestimations and underestimations suggest that these rules may be too broad or too restrictive, respectively.

4. **Potential for Further Optimization:**
   - The model may be underfitting, as it has not consistently achieved the highest possible accuracy, and misclassifications persist.
   - There is room to refine the rules to improve both support and accuracy without overfitting.

**Suggestions for Improving the Current Rules:**

1. **Refine Rule 2:**
   - **Issue:** Overestimation occurs due to broad categorization of electron-withdrawing and electron-donating groups.
   - **Suggestion:** 
     - Narrow the definition by specifying the exact nature and position of substituents on the aromatic ring.
     - Differentiate between electron-withdrawing groups that enhance catalyst activity and those that do not.
     - Consider the impact of ortho and meta substituents, not just para positions.
     - **Example Adjustment:** Specify that only strong electron-withdrawing groups at the para position, such as nitro or halogens, lead to high yield, while weakly withdrawing groups may not have the same effect.

2. **Adjust Rule 3:**
   - **Issue:** Underestimation in molecules with heteroaromatic rings due to strict criteria.
   - **Suggestion:**
     - Expand the rule to include a broader range of heteroaromatic systems.
     - Account for substituents on the heteroaromatic ring that may influence coordination with the metal center.
     - Include heterocycles with different heteroatoms (e.g., oxygen, sulfur) and varying nitrogen positions.

3. **Modify Rule 4:**
   - **Issue:** Overemphasis on medium-length aliphatic chains without considering additional functional groups.
   - **Suggestion:**
     - Introduce conditions that factor in the presence of polar or interfering groups on the hydrocarbon chain.
     - Assess the effect of terminal functional groups on the chain's ability to enhance catalyst activity.
     - **Example Adjustment:** Modify the rule to state that aliphatic carboxylic acids with unfunctionalized medium-length chains (6–8 carbons) lead to high yield, while the presence of additional polar groups may reduce yield.

4. **Reassess Rule 10:**
   - **Issue:** Overestimation due to not distinguishing between bulky groups that hinder or enhance catalyst interaction.
   - **Suggestion:**
     - Refine the rule to consider steric hindrance and the flexibility of linkages.
     - Incorporate parameters related to molecular size, shape, and the ability of the modifier to access the active site.
     - **Example Adjustment:** Specify that only modifiers with bulky groups that do not obstruct coordination with the metal center lead to high yield.

5. **Enhance Rule Specificity and Clarity:**
   - Use precise chemical language and standardized substructure patterns (SMARTS) to define rules clearly.
   - Avoid overlapping conditions between rules by clearly delineating the structural features associated with each rule.
   - Ensure that rules are mutually exclusive where appropriate to prevent conflicting contributions.

6. **Increase Support Without Sacrificing Confidence:**
   - Generalize certain rules where possible to cover more compounds.
   - Combine similar rules to create overarching rules that maintain high confidence.
   - Reevaluate the data to identify common features among high-yield modifiers that are not currently captured.

7. **Utilize SHAP Analysis for Targeted Refinement:**
   - Focus on molecules with the highest discrepancies between predicted and experimental yields.
   - Adjust rules that contribute significantly to misclassifications based on their SHAP values.
   - Iteratively refine rules and assess their impact on model predictions.

8. **Incorporate Additional Molecular Descriptors:**
   - Consider features such as:
     - **3D Conformation and Chirality:** Stereochemistry can affect how modifiers interact with the catalyst.
     - **Solubility and Lipophilicity:** These properties influence the modifier's availability in the reaction medium.
     - **Hydrogen Bonding Capacity:** Modifiers with multiple hydrogen bond donors or acceptors may coordinate differently.
     - **Donor-Acceptor Properties:** Electron-rich or electron-deficient sites in the modifier can impact coordination.

9. **Validate and Test Revised Rules:**
   - Apply the refined rules to a validation set to ensure they generalize well beyond the training data.
   - Monitor changes in accuracy, support, confidence, and lift to evaluate the effectiveness of adjustments.

10. **Engage Domain Experts:**
    - Collaborate with chemists or catalysis experts to provide insights that may not be evident from data analysis alone.
    - Verify that the chemical reasoning behind each rule aligns with known principles of coordination chemistry and catalysis.

**Assessment of Further Optimization Possibility**

Given the persistent misclassifications and areas identified for improvement, there is a clear possibility for further optimizing the current rules without overfitting. By refining the rules based on the suggestions above, we can aim to achieve higher accuracy, increased support, and better generalization to new data.

Final Metric Message:

    Target Name: yield
    GPT Model: o1-preview-2024-09-12
    ML Model: ETC
    Current_Rules:
    **Start of Rules**

- **Rule 1**: Modifiers that are **α-amino acids with additional carboxyl groups in the side chain**, specifically **α,β-dicarboxylic acids**, result in **High Yield**. The presence of both amino and multiple carboxyl groups enhances coordination with metal centers, improving catalyst activity. Examples include:

  - *(2S)-2-aminobutanedioic acid* (Aspartic acid)
  - *(2S)-2-aminopentanedioic acid* (Glutamic acid)

- **Rule 2**: Modifiers that are **aromatic carboxylic acids with electron-withdrawing groups at the para position** lead to **High Yield**. Electron-withdrawing groups increase acidity and strengthen interactions with metal ions. Examples:

  - *4-nitrobenzoic acid* (nitro group)
  - *4-(bromomethyl)benzoic acid* (bromomethyl group)

  Conversely, modifiers with **electron-donating groups at the para position** without additional coordinating functionalities result in **Low Yield**. Example:

  - *4-methylbenzoic acid* (methyl group)

  An exception is the **amino group**; despite being electron-donating, para-amino groups coordinate effectively with metals, leading to **High Yield**. Example:

  - *4-aminobenzoic acid*

- **Rule 3**: Modifiers that are **heteroaromatic carboxylic acids containing nitrogen atoms in the ring** result in **High Yield**. Nitrogen atoms coordinate with metal centers, enhancing catalyst performance. Examples:

  - *pyridine-3-carboxylic acid*
  - *1H-pyrazole-4-carboxylic acid*
  - *2-oxo-1H-pyridine-4-carboxylic acid*

  Modifiers where the heteroaromatic ring is connected via a flexible **aliphatic linker** also result in **High Yield** due to increased flexibility and coordination. Example:

  - *4-pyridin-4-ylbutanoic acid*

  In contrast, direct attachment of bulky heteroaromatic rings without a linker leads to **Low Yield**. Example:

  - *4-pyridin-4-ylbenzoic acid*

- **Rule 4**: Modifiers that are **aliphatic carboxylic acids with medium-length hydrocarbon chains (6–8 carbons)** lead to **High Yield**. The hydrophobic chain may enhance substrate accessibility or catalyst solubility. Example:

  - *octanoic acid*

  Introduction of **terminal polar groups** such as hydroxyl or amino groups converts them into **Low Yield** modifiers due to increased polarity and potential interference with catalyst interactions. Examples:

  - *7-hydroxyheptanoic acid*
  - *7-aminoheptanoic acid*
  - *8-aminooctanoic acid*

- **Rule 5**: Modifiers with **multiple hydroxyl groups adjacent to the carboxylic acid**, such as sugar acids, produce a **High Yield**. The numerous hydroxyl groups enhance hydrogen bonding and chelation with metal centers. Example:

  - *(2R,3S,4R,5R)-2,3,4,5,6-pentahydroxyhexanoic acid* (Gluconic acid)

- **Rule 6**: Modifiers containing **aliphatic thiol groups near the carboxylic acid** result in **High Yield** due to the strong coordinating ability of thiols with metal centers. Example:

  - *3-sulfanylpropanoic acid*

  However, when the thiol group is attached to an **aromatic ring**, the yield is **Low** due to reduced flexibility and possible steric hindrance. Example:

  - *4-sulfanylbenzoic acid*

- **Rule 7**: Modifiers that are **α-amino acids with large, polarizable aromatic side chains capable of π-stacking and hydrogen bonding interactions** result in **High Yield**. These interactions can stabilize the catalyst-substrate complex. Example:

  - *(2R)-2-amino-3-(1H-indol-3-yl)propanoic acid* (Tryptophan)

  In contrast, α-amino acids with **smaller aromatic side chains** lacking additional interaction sites result in **Low Yield**. Example:

  - *(2S)-2-amino-3-phenylpropanoic acid* (Phenylalanine)

- **Rule 8**: Modifiers featuring an **aromatic or heteroaromatic ring connected via a flexible aliphatic linker (3–5 carbons)** to the carboxyl group lead to **High Yield** due to enhanced flexibility and coordination with the catalyst. Examples:

  - *5-(4-hydroxyphenyl)pentanoic acid*
  - *4-pyridin-4-ylbutanoic acid*

  **Shorter linkers** or direct attachments without flexibility result in **Low Yield** due to steric hindrance. Example:

  - *3-(4-hydroxyphenyl)propanoic acid*

- **Rule 9**: Modifiers containing **strong electron-withdrawing groups** such as **perfluoroalkyl chains** directly attached to the carboxylic acid result in **Low Yield**. The electron-withdrawing effect reduces the modifier's ability to coordinate effectively with the catalyst. Example:

  - *2,2,3,3,4,4,5,5,5-nonafluoropentanoic acid*

- **Rule 10**: Modifiers with **bulky or polar functional groups attached via flexible ether or amide linkages** tend to result in **Low Yield**. Steric bulk and increased polarity may hinder proper orientation and coordination with the catalyst. Examples:

  - *2-[2-(2-methoxyethoxy)ethoxy]acetic acid*
  - *2-[(4-hydroxybenzoyl)amino]acetic acid*
  - *3-[2-(2-aminoethoxy)ethoxy]propanoic acid*

- **Rule 11**: Modifiers with **aldehyde groups adjacent to the carboxylic acid on aromatic rings** lead to **Low Yield**. Aldehyde groups may compete with the catalyst or participate in unwanted side reactions. Examples:

  - *4-formylbenzoic acid*
  - *5-formyl-2-hydroxybenzoic acid*

- **Rule 12**: Modifiers that are **amide-containing compounds or peptides with bulky or polar side chains**, especially those containing sulfur groups, tend to lead to **Low Yield**. Steric hindrance and competing interactions may interfere with catalyst function. Example:

  - *2-[[(2S)-2-amino-4-methylsulfanylbutanoyl]amino]ethanoic acid*

- **Rule 13**: Modifiers that are **dicarboxylic acids with carbonyl groups in the chain (e.g., keto acids)** result in **Low Yield**. The additional carbonyl groups may chelate metal centers differently, disrupting catalyst activity. Example:

  - *4-oxoheptanedioic acid*

- **Rule 14**: Modifiers that are **aromatic carboxylic acids with extended conjugation or fused ring systems** can result in **High Yield**, possibly due to enhanced π-interactions with the catalyst. Example:

  - *1,3-dioxo-2-benzofuran-5-carboxylic acid*

**End of Rules**---------------------------------------------------------------

Also, consider the impact of substituents at the **ortho and meta positions** by updating the rule to reflect that electron-withdrawing groups at these positions may have a different effect. Use precise SMARTS patterns to capture these specific substitution patterns.

- **Advice 2**: **Adjust Rule 3** to include a broader range of **heteroaromatic carboxylic acids**. Expand the rule to encompass heterocycles containing various heteroatoms (e.g., nitrogen, oxygen, sulfur) and different ring systems like **pyridines**, **pyrimidines**, **pyrazoles**, **imidazoles**, **thiophenes**, and **furans**. Incorporate modifiers with heteroaromatic rings that have substituents capable of coordinating with metal centers. Update the SMARTS patterns to include these heterocycles with varying positions of heteroatoms and substituents.

- **Advice 3**: **Modify Rule 4** to specify that only **aliphatic carboxylic acids with unfunctionalized medium-length hydrocarbon chains (6–8 carbons)** result in **High Yield**. Introduce a condition that the presence of **additional polar groups** (e.g., hydroxyl, amino, thiol) on the chain reduces yield, converting them into **Low Yield** modifiers. Update the rule's SMARTS pattern to exclude modifiers with polar functional groups attached to the hydrocarbon chain.

- **Advice 4**: **Reassess Rule 10** by refining it to account for **steric hindrance and flexibility**. Specify that modifiers with **bulky or polar functional groups** can lead to **High Yield** if they are connected via **flexible linkages** that allow proper orientation and coordination with the catalyst. Conversely, if the bulky groups are too close to the coordinating site or the linkages are rigid, they may hinder activity and result in **Low Yield**. Use SMARTS patterns to define the length and flexibility of linkers (e.g., specifying chain lengths or the presence of rotatable bonds).

- **Advice 5**: **Enhance the specificity and clarity of all rules** by utilizing **precise chemical language** and **standardized substructure patterns (SMARTS)**. **Start of Rules**

- **Rule 1**: Modifiers that are **α-amino acids with two carboxyl groups**, specifically **aspartic acid** and **glutamic acid**, result in **High Yield**. The presence of both an amino group and side-chain carboxyl group enhances coordination with metal centers, improving catalyst activity. Examples include:

  - *(2S)-2-aminobutanedioic acid* (Aspartic acid)
  - *(2S)-2-aminopentanedioic acid* (Glutamic acid)

- **Rule 2**: Modifiers that are **aromatic carboxylic acids with strong electron-withdrawing groups at the para position** lead to **High Yield**. Electron-withdrawing substituents increase the acidity of the carboxyl group, strengthening interactions with metal ions. Examples:

  - *4-nitrobenzoic acid* (nitro group)
  - *4-(bromomethyl)benzoic acid* (bromomethyl group)

  Conversely, modifiers with **electron-donating groups at the para position** result in **Low Yield** due to decreased acidity and weaker metal coordination. Example:

  - *4-methylbenzoic acid* (methyl group)

- **Rule 3**: Modifiers that are **aromatic carboxylic acids with amino groups at the para position** result in **High Yield**. The amino group can coordinate directly with metal centers, enhancing catalyst performance. Example:

  - *4-aminobenzoic acid*

- **Rule 4**: Modifiers that are **heteroaromatic carboxylic acids containing nitrogen atoms in the ring**, such as **pyridine** or **pyrazole rings**, result in **High Yield**. Nitrogen atoms coordinate effectively with metal centers. Examples:

  - *pyridine-3-carboxylic acid*
  - *1H-pyrazole-4-carboxylic acid*
  - *2-oxo-1H-pyridine-4-carboxylic acid*

- **Rule 5**: Modifiers featuring an **aromatic or heteroaromatic ring connected via a flexible aliphatic linker (3–5 carbons)** to the carboxyl group lead to **High Yield**. The linker provides flexibility, facilitating optimal coordination with the catalyst. Examples:

  - *5-(4-hydroxyphenyl)pentanoic acid*
  - *4-pyridin-4-ylbutanoic acid*

  In contrast, modifiers with **shorter linkers** or direct attachment result in **Low Yield** due to steric hindrance or reduced flexibility. Example:

  - *3-(4-hydroxyphenyl)propanoic acid*

- **Rule 6**: Modifiers that are **aliphatic carboxylic acids with medium-length hydrocarbon chains (6–8 carbons) lacking additional polar groups** result in **High Yield**. The hydrophobic chain may enhance substrate accessibility or catalyst interaction. Example:

  - *octanoic acid*

  Introducing **polar functional groups** such as hydroxyl or amino groups into the chain converts them into **Low Yield** modifiers due to increased polarity and potential interference with catalyst interactions. Examples:

  - *7-hydroxyheptanoic acid*
  - *7-aminoheptanoic acid*
  - *8-aminooctanoic acid*

- **Rule 7**: Modifiers containing **multiple hydroxyl groups adjacent to the carboxyl group**, such as **sugar acids**, produce a **High Yield**. The numerous hydroxyl groups enhance hydrogen bonding and chelation with metal centers. Example:

  - *(2R,3S,4R,5R)-2,3,4,5,6-pentahydroxyhexanoic acid* (Gluconic acid)

- **Rule 8**: Modifiers with **thiol groups near the carboxyl group** result in **High Yield** due to the strong coordinating ability of thiols with metal centers. Example:

  - *3-sulfanylpropanoic acid*

  However, when the thiol group is attached to an **aromatic ring**, the yield is **Low** due to reduced flexibility and possible steric hindrance. Example:

  - *4-sulfanylbenzoic acid*

- **Rule 9**: Modifiers that are **α-amino acids with large, polarizable aromatic side chains**, such as **tryptophan**, result in **High Yield**. These side chains can engage in π-stacking and hydrogen bonding, stabilizing the catalyst-substrate complex. Example:

  - *(2R)-2-amino-3-(1H-indol-3-yl)propanoic acid* (Tryptophan)

  In contrast, α-amino acids with **simple aromatic side chains** lacking additional interaction sites result in **Low Yield**. Example:

  - *(2S)-2-amino-3-phenylpropanoic acid* (Phenylalanine)

- **Rule 10**: Modifiers with **aldehyde groups adjacent to the carboxyl group on aromatic rings** lead to **Low Yield**. Aldehyde groups may compete with the catalyst or participate in unwanted side reactions. Examples:

  - *4-formylbenzoic acid*
  - *5-formyl-2-hydroxybenzoic acid*

- **Rule 11**: Modifiers containing **strong electron-withdrawing groups like perfluoroalkyl chains** directly attached to the carboxyl group result in **Low Yield**. The strong electron-withdrawing effect reduces the modifier's ability to coordinate effectively with the catalyst. Example:

  - *2,2,3,3,4,4,5,5,5-nonafluoropentanoic acid*

- **Rule 12**: Modifiers with **bulky or polar functional groups attached via flexible ether or amide linkages** tend to result in **Low Yield**. Steric bulk and increased polarity may hinder proper orientation and coordination with the catalyst. Examples:

  - *2-[2-(2-methoxyethoxy)ethoxy]acetic acid*
  - *2-[(4-hydroxybenzoyl)amino]acetic acid*
  - *3-[2-(2-aminoethoxy)ethoxy]propanoic acid*

- **Rule 13**: Modifiers that are **dicarboxylic acids with carbonyl groups in the chain (e.g., keto acids)** result in **Low Yield**. The additional carbonyl groups may chelate metal centers differently, disrupting catalyst activity. Example:

  - *4-oxoheptanedioic acid*

- **Rule 14**: Modifiers featuring **fused aromatic ring systems with carbonyl functionalities** can result in **High Yield** due to enhanced π-interactions and coordination sites. Example:

  - *1,3-dioxo-2-benzofuran-5-carboxylic acid*

- **Rule 15**: Modifiers that are **amide-containing compounds or peptides with bulky or polar side chains**, especially those containing sulfur groups, tend to lead to **Low Yield**. Steric hindrance and competing interactions may interfere with catalyst function. Example:

  - *2-[[(2S)-2-amino-4-methylsulfanylbutanoyl]amino]ethanoic acid*

- **Rule 1**: Modifiers that are **aromatic carboxylic acids with electron-withdrawing groups at the para position** lead to **High Yield**. Electron-withdrawing groups increase the acidity of the carboxyl group, enhancing coordination with metal ions. Examples:

  - *4-nitrobenzoic acid* (nitro group)
  - *4-(bromomethyl)benzoic acid* (bromomethyl group)

- **Rule 2**: Modifiers that are **aromatic carboxylic acids with electron-donating groups at the para position** result in **Low Yield**. Electron-donating groups reduce the acidity of the carboxyl group, weakening metal coordination. Examples:

  - *4-methylbenzoic acid* (methyl group)
  - *4-sulfanylbenzoic acid* (thiol group)

- **Rule 3**: Modifiers that are **heteroaromatic carboxylic acids containing nitrogen atoms in the ring**, such as **pyridine** or **pyrazole rings**, result in **High Yield**. Nitrogen atoms coordinate effectively with metal centers, enhancing catalyst activity. Examples:

  - *pyridine-3-carboxylic acid*
  - *1H-pyrazole-4-carboxylic acid*

- **Rule 4**: Modifiers with **aldehyde groups adjacent to the carboxyl group on aromatic rings** lead to **Low Yield**. Aldehyde groups can interfere with metal coordination or participate in side reactions. Examples:

  - *4-formylbenzoic acid*
  - *5-formyl-2-hydroxybenzoic acid*

- **Rule 5**: Modifiers that are **aliphatic carboxylic acids with medium-length hydrocarbon chains (6–8 carbons) lacking additional polar groups** result in **High Yield**. The hydrophobic chain may enhance substrate interaction with the catalyst. Example:

  - *octanoic acid*

- **Rule 6**: Modifiers that are **aliphatic carboxylic acids with medium-length chains (6–8 carbons) containing polar functional groups** such as hydroxyl or amino groups result in **Low Yield**. These polar groups may interfere with catalyst coordination. Examples:

  - *7-hydroxyheptanoic acid*
  - *7-aminoheptanoic acid*
  - *8-aminooctanoic acid*

- **Rule 7**: Modifiers that are **α-amino acids with additional carboxyl groups in the side chain**, such as **aspartic acid** and **glutamic acid**, result in **High Yield**. The presence of both amino and extra carboxyl groups provides multiple coordination sites. Examples:

  - *(2S)-2-aminobutanedioic acid* (Aspartic acid)
  - *(2S)-2-aminopentanedioic acid* (Glutamic acid)

- **Rule 8**: Modifiers that are **α-amino acids with large, polarizable aromatic side chains containing coordination-capable groups**, such as the indole ring in **tryptophan**, result in **High Yield**. These side chains facilitate π-stacking and additional coordination. Example:

  - *(2R)-2-amino-3-(1H-indol-3-yl)propanoic acid* (Tryptophan)

- **Rule 9**: Modifiers that are **α-amino acids with simple aromatic side chains lacking additional functional groups** result in **Low Yield**. Limited interaction sites reduce coordination with the catalyst. Example:

  - *(2S)-2-amino-3-phenylpropanoic acid* (Phenylalanine)

- **Rule 10**: Modifiers containing **thiol groups near the carboxyl group on aliphatic chains** result in **High Yield** due to the strong coordinating ability of thiol groups with metal centers. Example:

  - *3-sulfanylpropanoic acid*

- **Rule 11**: Modifiers containing **multiple hydroxyl groups adjacent to the carboxyl group**, such as **sugar acids**, result in **High Yield**. The hydroxyl groups enhance chelation with metal centers. Example:

  - *(2R,3S,4R,5R)-2,3,4,5,6-pentahydroxyhexanoic acid* (Gluconic acid)

- **Rule 12**: Modifiers with **bulky ether or amide linkages connecting polar functional groups** tend to result in **Low Yield**. Increased steric hindrance and polarity may disrupt coordination with the catalyst. Examples:

  - *2-[2-(2-methoxyethoxy)ethoxy]acetic acid*
  - *3-[2-(2-aminoethoxy)ethoxy]propanoic acid*

- **Rule 13**: Modifiers containing **perfluoroalkyl chains attached to the carboxyl group** result in **Low Yield**. Strong electron-withdrawing effects reduce the coordinating ability of the carboxyl group. Example:

  - *2,2,3,3,4,4,5,5,5-nonafluoropentanoic acid*

- **Rule 14**: Modifiers featuring an **aromatic or heteroaromatic ring connected via a flexible aliphatic linker (3–5 carbons)** to the carboxyl group lead to **High Yield**. The flexible linker allows optimal orientation for coordination. Examples:

  - *5-(4-hydroxyphenyl)pentanoic acid*
  - *4-pyridin-4-ylbutanoic acid*

  Conversely, modifiers with **shorter linkers (1–2 carbons)** result in **Low Yield** due to reduced flexibility. Example:

  - *3-(4-hydroxyphenyl)propanoic acid*

- **Rule 15**: Modifiers that are **dicarboxylic acids with additional keto groups in the chain (keto-dicarboxylic acids)** result in **Low Yield**. The keto group may form chelates that disrupt catalyst activity. Example:

  - *4-oxoheptanedioic acid*

**End of Rules**

Rule Advisor Message:
**Start of Advice**

- **Advice 1**: Simplify the rules by focusing on quantifiable molecular features that can be extracted from SMILES strings. Instead of using qualitative descriptions (e.g., "electron-withdrawing groups at the para position"), define rules based on numerical values of molecular descriptors such as Hammett sigma constants, which quantify electron-withdrawing/donating effects.

- **Advice 2**: Utilize cheminformatics tools to compute numerical descriptors for each modifier. Features like molecular weight, LogP (octanol-water partition coefficient), topological polar surface area (TPSA), counts of specific functional groups, and hydrogen bond donors/acceptors can be directly extracted from SMILES and used to create a numeric feature matrix.

- **Advice 3**: Reframe the rules to align with these quantifiable descriptors. For example, instead of stating "Modifiers that are aromatic carboxylic acids with electron-withdrawing groups at the para position lead to High Yield," specify a threshold value for the Hammett sigma constant or the electron-withdrawing parameter that correlates with high yield.

- **Advice 4**: Implement a scoring system where each modifier is evaluated based on the presence and count of beneficial or detrimental functional groups. Assign positive scores to features known to enhance yield (e.g., number of nitrogen atoms in heteroaromatic rings) and negative scores to features that reduce yield (e.g., number of aldehyde groups adjacent to carboxyl groups).

- **Advice 5**: Incorporate machine learning techniques to identify patterns and correlations in the dataset. Use statistical models to analyze how different numerical descriptors impact the yield, and adjust the rules to reflect these findings. This approach can also help uncover non-obvious relationships between molecular features and reaction outcomes.

- **Advice 6**: Group modifiers into clusters based on structural and physicochemical similarities using techniques like hierarchical clustering or principal component analysis (PCA). This can simplify the rule set by creating categories of modifiers that behave similarly in the reaction, making it easier to predict their effects on yield.

- **Advice 7**: Update the rules to include parameter ranges rather than specific functional groups. For instance, define rules such as "Modifiers with LogP values between X and Y tend to result in High Yield," which can be universally applied to any new modifier and easily computed from its SMILES representation.

- **Advice 8**: Validate and refine the new rules by cross-referencing them with the experimental data provided. Ensure that the rules accurately predict the yield outcomes in the dataset and adjust any discrepancies by revising the associated descriptor thresholds or including additional relevant features.

- **Advice 9**: Create a user-friendly guideline or algorithm that automates the evaluation of modifiers based on the new numerical rules. This tool can input SMILES strings and output predictions on the expected yield, streamlining the process for chemists and aiding in the design of new modifiers.

- **Advice 10**: Incorporate feedback mechanisms where the rules are continuously improved as more data becomes available. Establish a system for regularly updating the descriptors and thresholds based on the latest findings to keep the rule set current and increasingly accurate.

**End of Advice**

Rule Generator Message:
**Start of Rules**

- **Rule 1**: Modifiers that are **aromatic carboxylic acids with strong electron-withdrawing groups at the para position**, such as nitro or bromomethyl groups, lead to **High Yield**. Electron-withdrawing groups increase the acidity and coordinating ability of the carboxyl group, enhancing interaction with metal centers.

  Examples:
  - *4-nitrobenzoic acid* (para-nitro group)
  - *4-(bromomethyl)benzoic acid* (para-bromomethyl group)

- **Rule 2**: Modifiers that are **aromatic carboxylic acids with strong electron-donating groups at the para position capable of coordination**, such as amino groups, result in **High Yield**. The amino group can act as an additional coordination site, strengthening the interaction with the catalyst.

  Example:
  - *4-aminobenzoic acid* (para-amino group)

- **Rule 3**: Modifiers that are **aromatic carboxylic acids with electron-donating groups at the para position that are non-coordinating**, like methyl or thiol groups, result in **Low Yield**. These groups decrease the acidity of the carboxyl group without providing additional coordination sites, weakening interaction with the metal center.

  Examples:
  - *4-methylbenzoic acid* (para-methyl group)
  - *4-sulfanylbenzoic acid* (para-thiol group)

- **Rule 4**: Modifiers that are **heteroaromatic carboxylic acids containing nitrogen or oxygen atoms capable of chelation**, such as pyridine, pyrazole, or oxidized rings, result in **High Yield**. The heteroatoms enhance coordination with the metal center, improving catalyst activity.

  Examples:
  - *pyridine-3-carboxylic acid* (nicotinic acid)
  - *1H-pyrazole-4-carboxylic acid*
  - *2-oxo-1H-pyridine-4-carboxylic acid* (isonicotinic acid N-oxide)
  - *1,3-dioxo-2-benzofuran-5-carboxylic acid* (ketoisophthalic acid derivative)

- **Rule 5**: Modifiers that are **aromatic carboxylic acids with aldehyde groups adjacent to the carboxyl group** lead to **Low Yield**. Aldehyde groups can interfere with metal coordination or participate in side reactions, reducing catalyst effectiveness.

  Examples:
  - *4-formylbenzoic acid*
  - *5-formyl-2-hydroxybenzoic acid*

- **Rule 6**: Modifiers that are **α-amino acids with additional carboxyl groups in the side chain**, such as aspartic acid and glutamic acid, result in **High Yield**. The extra carboxyl groups provide multiple coordination sites, enhancing interaction with the metal catalyst.

  Examples:
  - *(2S)-2-aminobutanedioic acid* (aspartic acid)
  - *(2S)-2-aminopentanedioic acid* (glutamic acid)

- **Rule 7**: Modifiers that are **α-amino acids with aromatic side chains containing additional coordinating groups**, like the indole ring in tryptophan, lead to **High Yield**. The side chains offer π-stacking and coordination opportunities, strengthening catalyst interactions.

  Example:
  - *(2R)-2-amino-3-(1H-indol-3-yl)propanoic acid* (tryptophan)

- **Rule 8**: Modifiers that are **α-amino acids with non-coordinating side chains or sterically hindered functional groups** result in **Low Yield**. Limited interaction sites or steric hindrance reduce coordination with the catalyst.

  Examples:
  - *(2S)-2-amino-3-phenylpropanoic acid* (phenylalanine)
  - *(2S)-6-acetamido-2-aminohexanoic acid* (N-acetyl-L-lysine)

- **Rule 9**: Modifiers that are **aliphatic carboxylic acids with medium-length hydrocarbon chains (6–8 carbons) lacking additional polar groups** lead to **High Yield**. The hydrophobic chains may promote substrate accessibility to the catalyst's active sites without interfering with coordination.

  Example:
  - *octanoic acid*

- **Rule 10**: Modifiers that are **aliphatic carboxylic acids with polar functional groups (hydroxyl or amino) further along the chain** result in **Low Yield**. These polar groups may form competing interactions or disrupt catalyst structure, hindering reactivity.

  Examples:
  - *7-hydroxyheptanoic acid*
  - *7-aminoheptanoic acid*
  - *8-aminooctanoic acid*

- **Rule 11**: Modifiers containing **multiple hydroxyl groups adjacent to the carboxyl group**, such as **sugar acids**, result in **High Yield**. The numerous hydroxyls enhance chelation with metal centers, stabilizing the catalyst.

  Examples:
  - *(2R,3S,4R,5R)-2,3,4,5,6-pentahydroxyhexanoic acid* (gluconic acid)
  - *2-[[1,3-dihydroxy-2-(hydroxymethyl)propan-2-yl]oxy]acetic acid*

- **Rule 12**: Modifiers with **perfluoroalkyl chains attached to the carboxyl group** result in **Low Yield**. The strong electron-withdrawing effect reduces the coordinating ability of the carboxyl group, diminishing interaction with the catalyst.

  Example:
  - *2,2,3,3,4,4,5,5,5-nonafluoropentanoic acid*

- **Rule 13**: Modifiers featuring an **aromatic or heteroaromatic ring connected via a flexible aliphatic linker (3–5 carbons) to the carboxyl group** lead to **High Yield**. The flexible linker allows optimal orientation and interaction with the catalyst.

  Examples:
  - *5-(4-hydroxyphenyl)pentanoic acid*
  - *4-pyridin-4-ylbutanoic acid*

  Conversely, modifiers with **shorter linkers (1–2 carbons)** result in **Low Yield** due to reduced flexibility and suboptimal coordination.

  Example:
  - *3-(4-hydroxyphenyl)propanoic acid*

- **Rule 14**: Modifiers containing **thiol groups near the carboxyl group on aliphatic chains** result in **High Yield**. Thiol groups strongly coordinate with metal centers, enhancing catalyst stability.

  Example:
  - *3-sulfanylpropanoic acid*

  However, **thiol groups attached to aromatic rings** may lead to **Low Yield**, possibly due to steric hindrance or less effective coordination.

  Example:
  - *4-sulfanylbenzoic acid*

- **Rule 15**: Modifiers with **bulky ether or amide linkages connecting polar functional groups** tend to result in **Low Yield**. Increased steric hindrance and polarity may disrupt effective coordination with the catalyst.

  Examples:
  - *2-[2-(2-methoxyethoxy)ethoxy]acetic acid*
  - *3-[2-(2-aminoethoxy)ethoxy]propanoic acid*
  - *2-[[(2S)-2-amino-4-methylsulfanylbutanoyl]amino]acetic acid*

**End of Rules**

**Comments on the Accuracies of the Validation Set and Train Set:**

Based on the latest training results, the current 5-fold cross-validation accuracy is **71.43%**. This is somewhat lower than the highest accuracies achieved in previous iterations, which reached up to **85.71%**. The validation accuracies from previous runs were consistently around **76%**, with test accuracies ranging from **62.5%** to **75%**. The fluctuation in accuracy suggests that the current set of rules may not be consistently capturing the underlying patterns in the data, and there may be issues with overfitting or underfitting.

**Analysis Based on the SHAP Values Calculated on the Validation Set:**

To assess the effectiveness of the current rules and identify opportunities for improvement, we analyzed the SHAP values, focusing on molecules where the predicted loading differs from the experimental value. The SHAP values indicate the contribution of each rule to the model's prediction for each molecule. By examining these values, we can identify which rules may be contributing to misclassifications.

**4. General Observations from SHAP Analysis:**

- **Rule 4** often shows significant negative SHAP values in cases where the model predicts low loading, regardless of the experimental value. This suggests that **Rule 4** is strongly influencing the model's predictions and may be overly penalizing certain molecular features.

- **Rule 3** shows both positive and negative SHAP values, contributing to discrepancies in predictions. Its broad application may be causing inconsistent effects on different molecules.

- **Rule 10** sometimes has large negative SHAP values, as seen with *C(CCCC(=O)O)CCCN*, indicating it may be contributing to incorrect predictions in certain cases.

**Assessment of Overfitting/Underfitting:**

The decrease in accuracy compared to previous iterations and the inconsistent performance across the validation set suggest that the model may be overfitting to specific features captured by certain rules (e.g., **Rule 4** and **Rule 3**) and underfitting in terms of generalizing across diverse molecular structures. The strong influence of a few rules indicates that the model's predictions are heavily dependent on these rules, which may not accurately represent the dataset's underlying structure.

**Interpretation of Accuracy Improvements:**

The fluctuation in accuracy metrics across iterations suggests that the current rules are not consistently improving the model's performance. The accuracy of **71.43%** is below the highest achieved accuracy of **85.71%**, indicating that recent changes to the rules may not be effectively capturing the necessary features or may have introduced undesirable bias.

**Deriving Insights from SHAP Analysis:**

- **Rule Refinement Opportunities:**

  - **Rule 4**: Given its significant negative impact on molecules that should have high loading, **Rule 4** may need refinement to avoid over-penalizing certain structures. It currently states that modifiers with "heteroaromatic carboxylic acids containing nitrogen or oxygen atoms capable of chelation" result in high yield. However, it seems to have a strong negative contribution in some cases, suggesting the rule may be too restrictive or incorrectly applied.

  - **Rule 3**: The inconsistent contributions of **Rule 3** indicate that the distinction between electron-donating groups that are non-coordinating may not be adequately captured. Revisiting the definitions and examples within **Rule 3** could help clarify its application.

- **Generalization Improvement:**

  The model may benefit from rules that are more generalized or that encompass additional molecular features not currently considered. Including more nuanced considerations of steric hindrance, electronic effects, and functional group interactions could enhance the model's ability to generalize.

**Recommendations for Improving Current Rules:**

1. **Refine Rule 4:**

   **Issue:** Rule 4 is contributing significant negative SHAP values in cases where it should predict high loading.

   **Recommendation:** Reevaluate the criteria for **Rule 4** to ensure it accurately captures heteroaromatic carboxylic acids that enhance catalyst activity. Consider expanding the rule to include a broader range of heteroaromatic rings or adjusting the emphasis on certain heteroatoms.

2. **Adjust Rule 3:**

   **Issue:** Rule 3 shows inconsistent SHAP contributions, leading to mispredictions.

   **Recommendation:** Clarify the definition of "non-coordinating" electron-donating groups and reexamine the examples provided. Ensure that the rule accurately differentiates between groups that truly reduce catalyst interaction and those that may have a neutral or positive effect.

3. **Modify Rule 10:**

   **Issue:** Rule 10 sometimes contributes large negative SHAP values, potentially causing incorrect low predictions.

   **Recommendation:** Assess whether the presence of polar functional groups on aliphatic chains always leads to low yield or if exceptions exist. Update the rule to account for cases where such groups may not hinder catalyst activity.

4. **Incorporate Additional Molecular Features:**

   **Recommendation:** Introduce new rules that consider factors like:

   - Steric effects caused by bulky substituents near coordination sites.
   - The impact of multiple coordination sites within the same molecule.
   - Electronic effects due to conjugation or resonance structures.

5. **Enhance Rule Specificity and Flexibility:**

   **Recommendation:** Adjust overly broad rules to be more specific, reducing unintended penalization of certain molecules. Conversely, broaden overly narrow rules to capture a wider variety of beneficial features.

6. **Validate and Iterate:**

   **Recommendation:** After modifying the rules, perform additional iterations with cross-validation to assess the impact on accuracy. Use SHAP analysis in each iteration to identify any new issues arising from the changes.

**Conclusion:**

The current set of rules shows potential but requires refinement to improve model accuracy and predictive capabilities. By focusing on the specific rules contributing to misclassifications, we can adjust the model to better capture the complex relationships between molecular structures and catalyst loading. Iterative evaluation and adjustment, guided by both accuracy metrics and SHAP analysis, will help in developing a more robust and generalizable model.

Project Manager Message:
**Summary of Discussions and Directions for Optimizing Current Rules:**

The current set of rules for predicting the yield of the remote δ-C(sp³)–H bond functionalization reaction demonstrates high confidence and lift, indicating strong predictive power when the rule conditions are met. However, the low support suggests that these rules apply to a limited subset of the dataset, potentially limiting their practical utility.

**Key Points from the Discussion:**

1. **Feature Matrix Assignment Issues:**
   - There are inconsistencies in the assignment of modifiers to rules within the feature matrix, particularly concerning **Rule 4**.
   - Modifiers such as *1,3-dioxo-2-benzofuran-5-carboxylic acid* and *2-oxo-1H-pyridine-4-carboxylic acid* should match **Rule 4** but are currently not correctly assigned.
   - Misassignments result in significant negative SHAP values for molecules that should receive positive contributions, leading to incorrect predictions.

2. **Over-Penalization by Specific Rules:**
   - **Rule 4** is contributing substantial negative SHAP values in cases where it should predict high loading, suggesting it may be too restrictive or incorrectly applied.
   - **Rule 3** shows inconsistent SHAP contributions, indicating that its criteria may not accurately distinguish between modifiers that lead to high or low yield.
   - **Rule 10** occasionally contributes large negative SHAP values, potentially causing incorrect low predictions for certain molecules.

3. **Model Performance Metrics:**
   - The decrease in accuracy compared to previous iterations indicates potential overfitting or underfitting.
   - The fluctuation in validation accuracies suggests that the current rules may not consistently capture underlying patterns in the data.

**Suggestions for Improving Current Rules:**

1. **Review and Correct Feature Matrix Assignments:**
   - **Action:** Audit the feature matrix to ensure each modifier is correctly assigned to the appropriate rules based on their chemical structures.
   - **Focus:** Pay particular attention to **Rule 4** and modifiers involving heteroaromatic carboxylic acids capable of chelation.

2. **Refine Rule 4:**
   - **Issue:** Rule 4 may be too narrowly defined or incorrectly excluding compounds that should be included.
   - **Action:** Expand the criteria of **Rule 4** to include a broader range of heteroaromatic structures with chelating abilities, such as additional heterocycles.
   - **Clarify Definitions:** Ensure the rule accurately captures compounds with heteroatoms (e.g., nitrogen, oxygen) capable of coordinating with metal centers, even if they have additional functional groups.

3. **Adjust Rule 3:**
   - **Issue:** Inconsistent SHAP contributions suggest that Rule 3 may not be accurately identifying non-coordinating electron-donating groups.
   - **Action:** Reevaluate the types of electron-donating groups considered non-coordinating and refine the rule to better distinguish between groups that significantly impact catalyst interaction and those that do not.
   - **Update Examples:** Provide clearer examples and possibly subdivide the rule to account for different electron-donating groups.

4. **Modify Rule 10:**
   - **Issue:** Rule 10 may be incorrectly predicting low yields for modifiers with polar functional groups that do not necessarily hinder catalyst activity.
   - **Action:** Assess whether all polar functional groups on aliphatic chains adversely affect yield or if exceptions exist.
   - **Adjustment:** Revise the rule to account for cases where such functional groups do not interfere with catalyst interactions.

5. **Increase Rule Support:**
   - **Action:** Broaden the applicability of rules by generalizing conditions where appropriate without significantly compromising confidence and lift.
   - **Approach:** Merge overlapping rules, group similar functional groups, or create hierarchical rules that cover broader categories while maintaining specificity.

6. **Incorporate Additional Molecular Features:**
   - **Action:** Introduce new rules or modify existing ones to consider additional chemical descriptors, such as:
     - Steric hindrance from bulky substituents.
     - Electronic effects due to conjugation or resonance.
     - The presence of multiple coordination sites.
     - Solvent interactions or environmental factors.
   - **Benefit:** Capturing a wider range of molecular features can enhance predictive power and generalizability.

7. **Validate Rules with Cross-Validation and Test Data:**
   - **Action:** Use cross-validation techniques and evaluate model performance on separate test datasets to ensure the rules generalize well and avoid overfitting.
   - **Adjustment:** Use insights from validation results to refine rules further, focusing on areas with consistent mispredictions.

8. **Enhance Rule Specificity and Flexibility:**
   - **Action:** Refine overly broad or narrow rules to achieve a balance between specificity and generality.
   - **Benefit:** This ensures that rules are neither too restrictive (missing true positives) nor too general (including false positives).

9. **Collaborate with Domain Experts:**
   - **Action:** Engage with chemists and catalysis experts to validate the chemical plausibility of the rules and gain insights into the reaction mechanisms.
   - **Benefit:** Expert input can help adjust rules to better reflect underlying chemical principles, improving their applicability and accuracy.

**Possibility for Further Optimization Without Overfitting:**

There is a strong possibility for further optimizing the current rules without overfitting. By correcting feature matrix assignments, refining rule definitions, and broadening rule applicability, the model's predictive accuracy and generalizability can be improved. Careful iteration, guided by validation and SHAP analysis, will enhance model performance while minimizing overfitting risk.

Final Metric Message:

    Target Name: yield
    GPT Model: o1-preview-2024-09-12
    ML Model: ETC
    Current_Rules:
    **Start of Rules**

- **Rule 1**: Modifiers that are **aromatic carboxylic acids with strong electron-withdrawing groups at the para position**, such as nitro or bromomethyl groups, lead to **High Yield**. Electron-withdrawing groups increase the acidity and coordinating ability of the carboxyl group, enhancing interaction with metal centers.

  Examples:
  - *4-nitrobenzoic acid* (para-nitro group)
  - *4-(bromomethyl)benzoic acid* (para-bromomethyl group)

- **Rule 2**: Modifiers that are **aromatic carboxylic acids with strong electron-donating groups at the para position capable of coordination**, such as amino groups, result in **High Yield**. The amino group can act as an additional coordination site, strengthening the interaction with the catalyst.

  Example:
  - *4-aminobenzoic acid* (para-amino group)

- **Rule 3**: Modifiers that are **aromatic carboxylic acids with electron-donating groups at the para position that are non-coordinating**, like methyl or thiol groups, result in **Low Yield**. These groups decrease the acidity of the carboxyl group without providing additional coordination sites, weakening interaction with the metal center.

  Examples:
  - *4-methylbenzoic acid* (para-methyl group)
  - *4-sulfanylbenzoic acid* (para-thiol group)

- **Rule 4**: Modifiers that are **heteroaromatic carboxylic acids containing nitrogen or oxygen atoms capable of chelation**, such as pyridine, pyrazole, or oxidized rings, result in **High Yield**. The heteroatoms enhance coordination with the metal center, improving catalyst activity.

  Examples:
  - *pyridine-3-carboxylic acid* (nicotinic acid)
  - *1H-pyrazole-4-carboxylic acid*
  - *2-oxo-1H-pyridine-4-carboxylic acid* (isonicotinic acid N-oxide)
  - *1,3-dioxo-2-benzofuran-5-carboxylic acid* (ketoisophthalic acid derivative)

- **Rule 5**: Modifiers that are **aromatic carboxylic acids with aldehyde groups adjacent to the carboxyl group** lead to **Low Yield**. Aldehyde groups can interfere with metal coordination or participate in side reactions, reducing catalyst effectiveness.

  Examples:
  - *4-formylbenzoic acid*
  - *5-formyl-2-hydroxybenzoic acid*

- **Rule 6**: Modifiers that are **α-amino acids with additional carboxyl groups in the side chain**, such as aspartic acid and glutamic acid, result in **High Yield**. The extra carboxyl groups provide multiple coordination sites, enhancing interaction with the metal catalyst.

  Examples:
  - *(2S)-2-aminobutanedioic acid* (aspartic acid)
  - *(2S)-2-aminopentanedioic acid* (glutamic acid)

- **Rule 7**: Modifiers that are **α-amino acids with aromatic side chains containing additional coordinating groups**, like the indole ring in tryptophan, lead to **High Yield**. The side chains offer π-stacking and coordination opportunities, strengthening catalyst interactions.

  Example:
  - *(2R)-2-amino-3-(1H-indol-3-yl)propanoic acid* (tryptophan)

- **Rule 8**: Modifiers that are **α-amino acids with non-coordinating side chains or sterically hindered functional groups** result in **Low Yield**. Limited interaction sites or steric hindrance reduce coordination with the catalyst.

  Examples:
  - *(2S)-2-amino-3-phenylpropanoic acid* (phenylalanine)
  - *(2S)-6-acetamido-2-aminohexanoic acid* (N-acetyl-L-lysine)

- **Rule 9**: Modifiers that are **aliphatic carboxylic acids with medium-length hydrocarbon chains (6–8 carbons) lacking additional polar groups** lead to **High Yield**. The hydrophobic chains may promote substrate accessibility to the catalyst's active sites without interfering with coordination.

  Example:
  - *octanoic acid*

- **Rule 10**: Modifiers that are **aliphatic carboxylic acids with polar functional groups (hydroxyl or amino) further along the chain** result in **Low Yield**. These polar groups may form competing interactions or disrupt catalyst structure, hindering reactivity.

  Examples:
  - *7-hydroxyheptanoic acid*
  - *7-aminoheptanoic acid*
  - *8-aminooctanoic acid*

- **Rule 11**: Modifiers containing **multiple hydroxyl groups adjacent to the carboxyl group**, such as **sugar acids**, result in **High Yield**. The numerous hydroxyls enhance chelation with metal centers, stabilizing the catalyst.

  Examples:
  - *(2R,3S,4R,5R)-2,3,4,5,6-pentahydroxyhexanoic acid* (gluconic acid)
  - *2-[[1,3-dihydroxy-2-(hydroxymethyl)propan-2-yl]oxy]acetic acid*

- **Rule 12**: Modifiers with **perfluoroalkyl chains attached to the carboxyl group** result in **Low Yield**. The strong electron-withdrawing effect reduces the coordinating ability of the carboxyl group, diminishing interaction with the catalyst.

  Example:
  - *2,2,3,3,4,4,5,5,5-nonafluoropentanoic acid*

- **Rule 13**: Modifiers featuring an **aromatic or heteroaromatic ring connected via a flexible aliphatic linker (3–5 carbons) to the carboxyl group** lead to **High Yield**. The flexible linker allows optimal orientation and interaction with the catalyst.

  Examples:
  - *5-(4-hydroxyphenyl)pentanoic acid*
  - *4-pyridin-4-ylbutanoic acid*

  Conversely, modifiers with **shorter linkers (1–2 carbons)** result in **Low Yield** due to reduced flexibility and suboptimal coordination.

  Example:
  - *3-(4-hydroxyphenyl)propanoic acid*

- **Rule 14**: Modifiers containing **thiol groups near the carboxyl group on aliphatic chains** result in **High Yield**. Thiol groups strongly coordinate with metal centers, enhancing catalyst stability.

  Example:
  - *3-sulfanylpropanoic acid*

  However, **thiol groups attached to aromatic rings** may lead to **Low Yield**, possibly due to steric hindrance or less effective coordination.

  Example:
  - *4-sulfanylbenzoic acid*

- **Rule 15**: Modifiers with **bulky ether or amide linkages connecting polar functional groups** tend to result in **Low Yield**. Increased steric hindrance and polarity may disrupt effective coordination with the catalyst.

  Examples:
  - *2-[2-(2-methoxyethoxy)ethoxy]acetic acid*

Rule Generator Message:
**Start of Rules**

- **Rule 1**: Modifiers that are **aromatic carboxylic acids with para-substituents capable of coordinating to metal centers**, such as amino (-NH₂) or nitro (-NO₂) groups, result in **High Yield**. These groups enhance the coordination with metal ions in the catalyst, improving its activity.

  Examples:
  - *4-aminobenzoic acid* (para-amino group)
  - *4-nitrobenzoic acid* (para-nitro group)

- **Rule 2**: Modifiers that are **aromatic carboxylic acids with para-substituents that are non-coordinating or interfere with coordination**, such as methyl (-CH₃), sulfanyl (-SH), or formyl (-CHO) groups, result in **Low Yield**. These groups do not enhance metal coordination and may hinder catalyst effectiveness.

  Examples:
  - *4-methylbenzoic acid* (para-methyl group)
  - *4-sulfanylbenzoic acid* (para-thiol group)
  - *4-formylbenzoic acid* (para-formyl group)

- **Rule 3**: Modifiers that are **heteroaromatic carboxylic acids with heteroatoms positioned to coordinate with metal centers**, such as nitrogen or oxygen in favorable positions, result in **High Yield**. The heteroatoms enhance binding to the metal catalyst.

  Examples:
  - *pyridine-3-carboxylic acid* (nicotinic acid)
  - *1H-pyrazole-4-carboxylic acid*
  - *2-oxo-1H-pyridine-4-carboxylic acid* (isonicotinic acid N-oxide)

- **Rule 4**: Modifiers that are **heteroaromatic carboxylic acids where heteroatoms are not favorably positioned or are sterically hindered** result in **Low Yield**. Ineffective coordination reduces catalyst activity.

  Example:
  - *4-pyridin-4-ylbenzoic acid*

- **Rule 5**: Modifiers that are **α-amino acids with additional carboxyl groups in the side chain**, such as aspartic acid and glutamic acid, result in **High Yield**. Multiple carboxyl groups provide additional coordination sites with the metal catalyst.

  Examples:
  - *(2S)-2-aminobutanedioic acid* (aspartic acid)
  - *(2S)-2-aminopentanedioic acid* (glutamic acid)

- **Rule 6**: Modifiers that are **α-amino acids with aromatic side chains containing heteroatoms**, like the indole ring in tryptophan, lead to **High Yield**. The side chains offer additional coordination and π-π interactions with the catalyst.

  Example:
  - *(2R)-2-amino-3-(1H-indol-3-yl)propanoic acid* (tryptophan)

- **Rule 7**: Modifiers that are **α-amino acids with non-coordinating or bulky side chains**, such as phenylalanine or N-acetylated amino acids, result in **Low Yield**. Lack of additional coordination sites or steric hindrance reduces catalyst effectiveness.

  Examples:
  - *(2S)-2-amino-3-phenylpropanoic acid* (phenylalanine)
  - *(2S)-6-acetamido-2-aminohexanoic acid* (N-acetyl-L-lysine)

- **Rule 8**: Modifiers that are **aliphatic carboxylic acids with medium-length hydrocarbon chains (6–8 carbons) lacking additional polar functional groups** lead to **High Yield**. The hydrophobic chains may enhance substrate access to the catalyst without interfering with coordination.

  Example:
  - *octanoic acid*

- **Rule 9**: Modifiers that are **aliphatic carboxylic acids with polar functional groups (hydroxyl or amino) along the chain** result in **Low Yield**. These polar groups may form competing interactions or disrupt catalyst structure.

  Examples:
  - *7-hydroxyheptanoic acid*
  - *7-aminoheptanoic acid*
  - *8-aminooctanoic acid*

- **Rule 10**: Modifiers that are **aromatic carboxylic acids connected via a flexible aliphatic linker (4–5 carbons) to another coordinating group or aromatic ring** result in **High Yield**. The flexible linker allows optimal orientation for effective coordination.

  Examples:
  - *5-(4-hydroxyphenyl)pentanoic acid*
  - *4-pyridin-4-ylbutanoic acid*

  Conversely, modifiers with **shorter linkers (1–3 carbons)** result in **Low Yield** due to limited flexibility and suboptimal coordination.

  Example:
  - *3-(4-hydroxyphenyl)propanoic acid*

- **Rule 11**: Modifiers containing **multiple hydroxyl groups adjacent to the carboxyl group**, such as sugar acids, result in **High Yield**. The multiple hydroxyls enhance chelation with metal centers, stabilizing the catalyst.

  Example:
  - *(2R,3S,4R,5R)-2,3,4,5,6-pentahydroxyhexanoic acid* (gluconic acid)

- **Rule 12**: Modifiers with **bulky ether or flexible polar functional groups in the aliphatic chain** result in **Low Yield**. Steric hindrance and increased polarity may disrupt effective coordination with the catalyst.

  Examples:
  - *2-[2-(2-methoxyethoxy)ethoxy]acetic acid*
  - *3-[2-(2-aminoethoxy)ethoxy]propanoic acid*

- **Rule 13**: Modifiers with **perfluoroalkyl chains attached to the carboxyl group** result in **Low Yield**. The strong electron-withdrawing effect reduces the coordinating ability of the carboxyl group.

  Example:
  - *2,2,3,3,4,4,5,5,5-nonafluoropentanoic acid*

- **Rule 14**: Modifiers containing **thiol groups near the carboxyl group on aliphatic chains** result in **High Yield**. Thiol groups strongly coordinate with metal centers, enhancing catalyst stability.

  Example:
  - *3-sulfanylpropanoic acid*

  However, **thiol groups attached to aromatic rings** may lead to **Low Yield**, possibly due to steric hindrance or less effective coordination.

  Example:
  - *4-sulfanylbenzoic acid*

- **Rule 15**: Modifiers with **aldehyde groups adjacent to the carboxyl group** result in **Low Yield**. Aldehyde groups can interfere with metal coordination or participate in side reactions, reducing catalyst effectiveness.

  Examples:
  - *4-formylbenzoic acid*
  - *5-formyl-2-hydroxybenzoic acid*

**End of Rules**

ML Commenter Message:
**Analysis of Current Model Performance:**

**1. Accuracy Evaluation:**

In the latest iteration, the classification model achieved a **5-fold validation accuracy of 77.14%**. This is comparable to the previous reference accuracies, which consistently hovered around 76% for validation and ranged from 62.5% to 75% for test accuracies. The slight improvement suggests that the current set of rules is performing on par with, or marginally better than, previous iterations.

**2. Overfitting/Underfitting Assessment:**

The consistent performance across multiple iterations, without significant gains in accuracy, indicates that the model might be **underfitting**. This suggests that the current rules are not capturing all the necessary features influencing the catalyst's performance. There is no evidence of overfitting since validation accuracy does not significantly exceed test accuracy, but the stability in moderate accuracy levels points toward underfitting.

**3. SHAP Analysis and Discrepancies:**

By examining the SHAP values for molecules where the predicted loading does not match the experimental value, we can identify which rules are contributing most to mispredictions.

- **Molecule: C(CC(=O)O)[C@@H](C(=O)O)N**

  - **Predicted Loading:** Low
  - **Experimental Value:** High
  - **SHAP Values:**
    - Rule1: **-0.0519**
    - Rule3: **-0.0754**
    - Rule8: **-0.0274**
    - Rule9: +0.0272
    - Rule10: -0.0152
    - Rule11: **-0.0275**

  **Analysis:** Significant negative SHAP values from **Rule3** and **Rule1** are contributing to the underestimation. Rule9 provides a positive contribution but is insufficient to offset the negatives.

- **Molecule: C1=CC(=CC=C1CBr)C(=O)O**

  - **Predicted Loading:** Low
  - **Experimental Value:** High
  - **SHAP Values:** (Similar pattern as above)

  **Analysis:** Again, **Rule3** and **Rule1** have significant negative contributions leading to misclassification.

- **Molecule: C([C@@H](C(=O)O)N)C(=O)O**

  - **Predicted Loading:** Low
  - **Experimental Value:** High
  - **SHAP Values:**
    - Rule1: **-0.0588**
    - Rule3: **-0.0836**
    - Rule8: **-0.0323**
    - Rule9: +0.0220
    - Rule10: **-0.0530**
    - Rule11: **-0.0587**

  **Analysis:** Negative contributions from **Rule3**, **Rule1**, **Rule11**, and **Rule10** are significant in the underestimation.

In instances where the experimental value is high but the predicted loading is low, **Rule3** consistently shows a significant negative SHAP value, indicating it heavily influences the incorrect low prediction.

**4. Interpretation of Accuracy Improvements:**

Given that the accuracy has plateaued at around 77%, it suggests that the model's performance is not improving significantly with the current set of rules. This stagnation implies that a local minimum may have been reached and that the current rules are insufficient to capture the complexity of the data fully.

**5. Insights from SHAP Analysis:**

The SHAP analysis highlights that **Rule3** and **Rule1** often contribute negatively in cases where the model underestimates the catalyst loading. This suggests that these rules may be too restrictive or not adequately capturing the characteristics of molecules that should yield high loading.

Conversely, there are fewer cases where overestimations occur, and when they do, the SHAP values do not point to specific rules with high positive contributions requiring adjustment.

---

**Comments on the Performance of Current Rules:**

The current rules provide a moderate level of accuracy but appear to be underfitting the data. The consistent misclassifications, particularly in underestimating high-loading modifiers, indicate that the rules may be overly stringent or not inclusive of all relevant chemical features influencing catalyst performance.

---

**Recommendations for Improvement:**

1. **Refine Rule 3:**

   - **Issue Identified:** **Rule 3** consistently contributes significant negative SHAP values in cases where the experimental loading is high, but the model predicts low loading.
   - **Action:** Re-express Rule 3 to broaden its scope. Currently, it might be too restrictive in identifying heteroaromatic carboxylic acids with favorably positioned heteroatoms. Consider including modifiers where heteroatoms are present but may not fit the original positional criteria, or adjust the rule to account for additional coordinating capabilities of such heteroatoms.

2. **Adjust Rule 1:**

   - **Issue Identified:** **Rule 1** also shows significant negative contributions in misclassifications.
   - **Action:** Review the specifics of Rule 1 to ensure that it accurately captures modifiers with aromatic carboxylic acids and para-substituents capable of coordinating with metal centers. It may be too narrow in its current form. Consider including other functional groups or positional isomers that can enhance coordination.

3. **Examine Rule 11 and Rule 10:**

   - **Issue Identified:** In some misclassifications, **Rule 11** and **Rule 10** contribute negatively.
   - **Action:** Evaluate these rules to determine if they are too general or not accounting for exceptions. Refining them may help improve predictions for specific molecules.

4. **Incorporate Additional Chemical Features:**

   - **Issue Identified:** The model may not be considering all relevant molecular features, leading to underfitting.
   - **Action:** Consider integrating additional descriptors into the feature matrix, such as electronic properties (e.g., electronegativity, electronic distribution), steric factors (e.g., molecular volume, surface area), or other functional groups that may influence coordination with the catalyst.

5. **Enhance Rule Generalization:**

   - **Issue Identified:** The rules may be over-specific, failing to generalize across different molecular structures.
   - **Action:** Modify the rules to be more inclusive, capturing a broader range of modifiers that can lead to high catalyst loading. Ensure that rules are not excluding valid candidates due to overly strict criteria.

6. **Iterative Refinement Cycle:**

   - **Issue Identified:** The iterative process may not have reached convergence.
   - **Action:** Continue refining the rules based on the feedback from the SHAP analysis. Focus on one or two rules at a time to assess the impact of changes on model performance. Monitor changes in accuracy to determine if refinements lead to improvements.

7. **Add New Rules if Necessary:**

   - **Issue Identified:** Existing rules may not cover all patterns in the data.
   - **Action:** Based on the analysis, consider formulating new rules that address observed patterns in misclassifications. For example, if certain functional groups or structural motifs are present in misclassified molecules, develop rules that account for their impact on catalyst loading.

---

**Conclusion:**

The current rules provide a foundation but require further refinement to improve the model's predictive capabilities. The SHAP analysis points to specific rules that, when adjusted, may reduce misclassifications. By refining these rules and potentially incorporating new ones, we can enhance the model's ability to generalize and more accurately predict catalyst performance. Continued iterations, guided by SHAP insights, will be crucial in achieving better alignment between predictions and experimental outcomes.

Project Manager Message:
**Summary of Discussions and Directions for Optimization:**

The current set of rules for predicting the yield of the remote δ-C(sp³)–H bond functionalization reaction demonstrates high accuracy in terms of confidence and lift metrics. Specifically, the rules achieve a training confidence of **98.33%** and a lift of **1.97**, indicating a strong association between the rule conditions and the predicted outcomes.

However, several areas need improvement to enhance the model's overall performance:

1. **Low Support:**
   - **Issue:** The current rules apply to only **5.33%** of the training data, meaning they cover a small portion of the dataset.
   - **Recommendation:** Adjust the rules to increase their applicability without sacrificing confidence and lift. This involves generalizing certain rule conditions to include more modifier types while maintaining predictive accuracy.

2. **Potential Underfitting:**
   - **Issue:** The validation accuracy has plateaued around **77%**, suggesting that the model might be underfitting and not capturing all relevant features.
   - **Recommendation:** Incorporate additional molecular descriptors and features into the model to better represent the chemistry influencing yield. This could include electronic properties, steric factors, and other functional groups that affect coordination with the catalyst.

3. **Refinement Based on SHAP Analysis:**
   - The SHAP (SHapley Additive exPlanations) analysis identifies specific rules contributing negatively to misclassifications:

   - **Rule 3 (Heteroaromatic Carboxylic Acids):**
     - **Issue:** Consistently contributes negative SHAP values in cases where the experimental yield is high but predicted as low. It may be too restrictive, excluding valid modifiers due to strict positional criteria of heteroatoms.
     - **Action:** Broaden Rule 3 to include heteroaromatic carboxylic acids with heteroatoms in various positions that can still coordinate effectively with the metal catalyst. Consider including modifiers with different heteroatoms or additional coordinating sites.

   - **Rule 1 (Aromatic Carboxylic Acids with Para-Substituents):**
     - **Issue:** Shows significant negative contributions in misclassifications, possibly due to narrow definitions of coordinating groups.
     - **Action:** Expand Rule 1 to incorporate a wider range of para-substituents capable of coordinating with metal centers. Include functional groups like hydroxyl (-OH), carboxyl (-COOH), or other nitrogen-containing groups that can enhance coordination.

   - **Rule 10 and Rule 11:**
     - **Issue:** Occasionally contribute negatively in predictions, indicating they might be overgeneralized or missing exceptions.
     - **Action:** Review these rules to identify any overgeneralization. Adjust them to account for specific cases where they might inaccurately predict the yield, possibly by introducing additional criteria or exceptions.

4. **Enhance Rule Flexibility:**
   - **Modify Rule Conditions:**
     - Slightly relax overly strict conditions in the rules to include more modifiers without significantly compromising accuracy.
   - **Combine Overlapping Rules:**
     - Identify rules with overlapping conditions and consider merging them to simplify the rule set and increase support.
   - **Develop New Rules:**
     - Analyze misclassified modifiers to identify patterns not covered by existing rules. Create new rules to capture these patterns and improve predictive performance.

5. **Incorporate Additional Chemical Features:**
   - **Issue:** The model may not fully capture the complexity of chemical interactions influencing yield.
   - **Action:** Introduce additional descriptors such as:
     - **Electronic Properties:** Electron density, electronegativity, or resonance effects that affect metal coordination.
     - **Steric Factors:** Molecular size, shape, and steric hindrance that influence access to the catalyst's active site.
     - **Hydrogen Bonding Capability:** Functional groups that can participate in hydrogen bonding, affecting catalyst interactions.

6. **Validate and Iterate:**
   - **Issue:** The risk of overfitting increases with continuous refinement on the training data.
   - **Action:** After adjusting the rules, validate the updated model on a separate test dataset to ensure improvements generalize beyond the training data.
   - **Iterative Refinement:**
     - Use insights from validation results to further fine-tune the rules.
     - Engage domain experts to review the chemical plausibility of the adjusted rules.

**Judgment on Further Optimization Possibility:**

There is significant potential for further optimization of the current rules without overfitting. By refining existing rules, introducing new ones based on observed patterns, and incorporating additional molecular features, we can enhance the model's accuracy and applicability. The SHAP analysis provides clear guidance on which rules require adjustments, enabling targeted improvements that will likely result in better predictive performance.

Final Metric Message:

    Target Name: yield
    GPT Model: o1-preview-2024-09-12
    ML Model: ETC
    Current_Rules:
    **Start of Rules**

- **Rule 1**: Modifiers that are **aromatic carboxylic acids with para-substituents capable of coordinating to metal centers**, such as amino (-NH₂) or nitro (-NO₂) groups, result in **High Yield**. These groups enhance the coordination with metal ions in the catalyst, improving its activity.

  Examples:
  - *4-aminobenzoic acid* (para-amino group)
  - *4-nitrobenzoic acid* (para-nitro group)

- **Rule 2**: Modifiers that are **aromatic carboxylic acids with para-substituents that are non-coordinating or interfere with coordination**, such as methyl (-CH₃), sulfanyl (-SH), or formyl (-CHO) groups, result in **Low Yield**. These groups do not enhance metal coordination and may hinder catalyst effectiveness.

  Examples:
  - *4-methylbenzoic acid* (para-methyl group)
  - *4-sulfanylbenzoic acid* (para-thiol group)
  - *4-formylbenzoic acid* (para-formyl group)

- **Rule 3**: Modifiers that are **heteroaromatic carboxylic acids with heteroatoms positioned to coordinate with metal centers**, such as nitrogen or oxygen in favorable positions, result in **High Yield**. The heteroatoms enhance binding to the metal catalyst.

  Examples:
  - *pyridine-3-carboxylic acid* (nicotinic acid)
  - *1H-pyrazole-4-carboxylic acid*
  - *2-oxo-1H-pyridine-4-carboxylic acid* (isonicotinic acid N-oxide)

- **Rule 4**: Modifiers that are **heteroaromatic carboxylic acids where heteroatoms are not favorably positioned or are sterically hindered** result in **Low Yield**. Ineffective coordination reduces catalyst activity.

  Example:
  - *4-pyridin-4-ylbenzoic acid*

- **Rule 5**: Modifiers that are **α-amino acids with additional carboxyl groups in the side chain**, such as aspartic acid and glutamic acid, result in **High Yield**. Multiple carboxyl groups provide additional coordination sites with the metal catalyst.

  Examples:
  - *(2S)-2-aminobutanedioic acid* (aspartic acid)
  - *(2S)-2-aminopentanedioic acid* (glutamic acid)

- **Rule 6**: Modifiers that are **α-amino acids with aromatic side chains containing heteroatoms**, like the indole ring in tryptophan, lead to **High Yield**. The side chains offer additional coordination and π-π interactions with the catalyst.

  Example:
  - *(2R)-2-amino-3-(1H-indol-3-yl)propanoic acid* (tryptophan)

- **Rule 7**: Modifiers that are **α-amino acids with non-coordinating or bulky side chains**, such as phenylalanine or N-acetylated amino acids, result in **Low Yield**. Lack of additional coordination sites or steric hindrance reduces catalyst effectiveness.

  Examples:
  - *(2S)-2-amino-3-phenylpropanoic acid* (phenylalanine)
  - *(2S)-6-acetamido-2-aminohexanoic acid* (N-acetyl-L-lysine)

- **Rule 8**: Modifiers that are **aliphatic carboxylic acids with medium-length hydrocarbon chains (6–8 carbons) lacking additional polar functional groups** lead to **High Yield**. The hydrophobic chains may enhance substrate access to the catalyst without interfering with coordination.

  Example:
  - *octanoic acid*

- **Rule 9**: Modifiers that are **aliphatic carboxylic acids with polar functional groups (hydroxyl or amino) along the chain** result in **Low Yield**. These polar groups may form competing interactions or disrupt catalyst structure.

  Examples:
  - *7-hydroxyheptanoic acid*
  - *7-aminoheptanoic acid*
  - *8-aminooctanoic acid*

- **Rule 10**: Modifiers that are **aromatic carboxylic acids connected via a flexible aliphatic linker (4–5 carbons) to another coordinating group or aromatic ring** result in **High Yield**. The flexible linker allows optimal orientation for effective coordination.

  Examples:
  - *5-(4-hydroxyphenyl)pentanoic acid*
  - *4-pyridin-4-ylbutanoic acid*

  Conversely, modifiers with **shorter linkers (1–3 carbons)** result in **Low Yield** due to limited flexibility and suboptimal coordination.

  Example:
  - *3-(4-hydroxyphenyl)propanoic acid*

- **Rule 11**: Modifiers containing **multiple hydroxyl groups adjacent to the carboxyl group**, such as sugar acids, result in **High Yield**. The multiple hydroxyls enhance chelation with metal centers, stabilizing the catalyst.

  Example:
  - *(2R,3S,4R,5R)-2,3,4,5,6-pentahydroxyhexanoic acid* (gluconic acid)

- **Rule 12**: Modifiers with **bulky ether or flexible polar functional groups in the aliphatic chain** result in **Low Yield**. Steric hindrance and increased polarity may disrupt effective coordination with the catalyst.

  Examples:
  - *2-[2-(2-methoxyethoxy)ethoxy]acetic acid*
  - *3-[2-(2-aminoethoxy)ethoxy]propanoic acid*

- **Rule 13**: Modifiers with **perfluoroalkyl chains attached to the carboxyl group** result in **Low Yield**. The strong electron-withdrawing effect reduces the coordinating ability of the carboxyl group.

  Example:
  - *2,2,3,3,4,4,5,5,5-nonafluoropentanoic acid*

- **Rule 14**: Modifiers containing **thiol groups near the carboxyl group on aliphatic chains** result in **High Yield**. Thiol groups strongly coordinate with metal centers, enhancing catalyst stability.

  Example:
  - *3-sulfanylpropanoic acid*

  However, **thiol groups attached to aromatic rings** may lead to **Low Yield**, possibly due to steric hindrance or less effective coordination.

  Example:
  - *4-sulfanylbenzoic acid*

- **Rule 15**: Modifiers with **aldehyde groups adjacent to the carboxyl group** result in **Low Yield**. Aldehyde groups can interfere with metal coordination or participate in side reactions, reducing catalyst effectiveness.

  Examples:
  - *4-formylbenzoic acid*
  - *5-formyl-2-hydroxybenzoic acid*
